Divine Attributes

5 min
The Division Argument: Unity Is Not Existence
Proof that unity ≠ existence via division reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core rational proof that unity is not identical to existence, based on the absurd consequence of division.

Core Idea
If unity were existence, splitting a body would annihilate it—an absurdity.

This lesson presents the main argument from the text: some thought unity and existence are identical, but the author refutes this by considering what happens when a unified body is divided. The absurd implication shows that unity must be distinct from existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a refutation, an example, and a conclusion.
The passage uses a conditional argument: if X then Y, but Y is false, so X is false.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
The claim is that unity is identical to existence. The refutation assumes this and shows that dividing a body would then be annihilation.
The refutation is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the absurdity
The example of splitting the ocean with a mosquito needle shows that division does not annihilate; therefore the assumption is false.
The author emphasizes that this is a necessary judgment, not just improbable.
4
Consider the objection
The author mentions that some might allow the absurdity based on improbability, but he dismisses such people as irrational.
This foreshadows the deeper objection addressed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود لشيء واحد من جهتين ولنا أن نقول : الوحدة عارضة للكثرة بالذات، وللكثير بالعرض، ولأجل ذلك التساوي الذي بينهما ( ظن بعضهم أنها ) أي الوحدة (نفس الوجود)، فتكون الوحدة الشخصية نفس الوجود الشخصية الثابت لكل موجود معين . ( ويبطله : أنه لو كان الوجود ) الشخصي نفس الوحدة ) الشخصي ( لكان التفريق ) الواقع في الجسم الواحد (إعداماً ) لذلك الجسم المشخص، وإيجاداً لجسمين آخرين إذ بالتفريق تبطل الوحدة المخصوصة فيبطل الوجود المخصوص ( وإنه ) أي كون التفريق إعداماً (باطل، إذ ليس شق البعوض بإبرته البحر الأخضر إعداماً له وإيجاداً لبحرین آخرین ضرورة والمجوز لذلك ) بناء على أنه مجرد استبعاد لا ينافي الجواز، مكابر) لمقتضى عقله ( لا يخاطب ) ولا يناظر. وإنما جوزه من جوزه بناء على أن الصورة الجسمية هوية متصلة في حد ذاتها، فإذا ورد عليها الانفصال زالت تلك الهوية الانفصالية، ووجد هويتان أخريان ،اتصاليتان والموجود في الحالتين معاً هو الهيولى التي لا اتصال لها في نفسها ولا انفصال بل تجامع كلا منهما، وه هي . قوله : ( ولنا أن نقول إلخ فمعنى قولنا : كل كثير واحد أعم من أن يكون موصوفاً بالوحدة بالذات أو بالعرض، وإنما أخر هذا الجواب مع موافقته لظاهر عبارة المصنف، لأن القول بأن الوحدة غير عارضة لذات الكثير، وإنما يوصف بها باعتبار الكثرة القائمة به، فمعنى قولنا الكثير واحد كثرته خلاف الظاهر والوجدان . قوله : ( ولأجل ذلك إلخ) ليس منشأ الظن مطرداً ، فلا يرد أنه يلزم من ذلك أن يظن الاتحاد بين كل متساويين كما وهم . قوله : ( فتكون إلخ) زاد هذا التفريع ليتوجه الإبطال المذكور. قوله : ( فيبطل إلخ) بناء على فرض الاتحاد بينهما . قوله : ( مكابر لمقتضى عقله فإن العقل الصريح يحكم بالفرق بين التفريق والإعدام، فإن من يقول : أعطني ماء من هذا الكوز ليس مقصوده أعدم ذلك الماء وأوجد ماء آخر. قوله : ( وإنما جوزه إلخ) بيان لمنشأ التجويز تتميماً للكلام، وليس غرضه دفع كون التجويز المذكور مكابرة، فإنه لا يندفع بذلك. قوله ( لكل موجود (معين قيد بالمعين ليخرج الطبائع عند من يقول بوجودها . قوله : إعداماً له وإيجاداً لبحرين آخرين قيل يمكن حمل كلام المصنف على أن التفريق حينئذ يكون إعداماً بالكلية وإيجاداً لبحرين من كتم العدم ابتداء بلا بقاء محل من الأول، وإلا فالباقي فرضاً أعني الهيولى قد بطل وحدته العرضية بسبب الصورة، فعلى تقدير أن تكون الوحدة الشخصية نفس الوجود الشخصي ينبغي أن يتقدم هو أيضاً، فيطابق كلامه مذهب الحكيم، وليس بشيء لما سبق إليه الإشارة من أن الوحدة الشخصية للهيولي محفوظة عندهم بالوحدة النوعية للصورة، لا بالوحدة الشخصية لها فلم تبطل الوحدة الشخصية للهيولى في البحر المشقوق، على أن قوله : والمجوز إلخ يأبى عنه نوع إباء . قوله : ( والموجود في الحالتين إلخ) كيلا يكون التفريق إعداماً بالكلية، كما لزم ذلك للنافين للهيولى القائلين بأن الجسم حقيقية هو الاتصال الجوهري فقط ولا يخفى على المنصف أن التفريق كما أنه ليس إعداماً بالكلية ليس إعداماً باعتبار بعض الأجزاء، فإن العقل يحكم بأن الماء بعد التفريق هو الماء السابق، إلا أنه زال منه الوحدة، وعرض له الكثرة . قوله دون هويته الشخصية بناء على أن الوحدة ليست من المشخصات، وإذا قال الحكماد ببقاء هيولى العناصر بالتشخص مع تكثرها باعتبار الأجسام العنصرية . قوله : ( أن الأمور لكلية أي المفهومات الموصوفة بالكلية في أنفسها موصوفة بالوحدة دون التشخص إذ تشخصها بعد عروض المشخصات.
The Fourth Observatory - First Objective: Unity is concomitant with existence for one thing from two aspects. And we may say: Unity is an accident of multiplicity essentially, and of the many accidentally. And because of the equality between them, some thought that it (i.e., unity) is identical to existence, so that individual unity is the individual existence established for every determinate existent. And it is refuted by: that if existence (individual) were identical to unity (individual), then division (occurring in one body) would be annihilation (of that individualized body) and bringing into existence of two other bodies, because by division the specific unity is annulled, so the specific existence is annulled. And that (i.e., that division is annihilation) is false, since the splitting of the ocean with a mosquito's needle is not annihilation of it and bringing into existence of two other oceans, necessarily. And the one who permits that (based on it being mere improbability not contradicting permissibility) is a contravener of the requirement of his intellect, not to be addressed nor argued with. But whoever permitted it did so based on the view that the corporeal form is a continuous identity in itself, so when separation occurs upon it, that disjunctive identity ceases, and two other continuous identities come into existence, and what exists in both states is the hyle, which has neither connection nor disconnection in itself, but accompanies each of them. That is it. His statement: (And we may say, etc.) So the meaning of our statement: every many is one, whether it is described with unity essentially or accidentally. And he delayed this answer despite its agreement with the apparent wording of the author, because the statement that unity is not an accident of the essence of the many, but is described with it by consideration of the multiplicity subsisting in it, so the meaning of our statement 'the many is one' is against the apparent and common perception. His statement: (And because of that equality, etc.) The source of the opinion is not universal, so it does not follow that one would suppose union between every two equals, as was imagined. His statement: (So it would be, etc.) He added this derivation to direct the mentioned refutation. His statement: (So through division he becomes annulled, etc.) Based on the assumption of union between them. His statement: (Contravener of the requirement of his intellect) For clear intellect judges the difference between dividing and annihilating: he who says 'give me water from this jug' does not intend to annihilate that water and bring another water. His statement: (And whoever permitted it, etc.) An explanation of the source of permissibility, a completion of the discourse, not intending to refute the mentioned permissibility being contumacy, for it is not repelled by that. His statement: (For every determinate existent) He qualified with 'determinate' to exclude universals according to those who affirm their existence. His statement: 'annihilation of it and bringing into existence of two other seas' It was said that the author's statement can be interpreted such that division then would be total annihilation and bringing into existence of two seas from absolute non-existence initially without any remainder from the first; otherwise the remainder hypothetically, i.e., the hyle, its accidental unity is annulled due to the form, so on the supposition that individual unity is individual existence, it too should be prior, so his statement would agree with the philosopher's doctrine—but it is nothing, due to the indication previously mentioned that the individual unity of the hyle is preserved by them through the specific unity of the form, not through its individual unity, so the individual unity of the hyle is not annulled in the split sea, and indeed his statement: 'and the one who permits it, etc.' somewhat rejects it. His statement: (And what exists in both states, etc.) So that division is not total annihilation, as was necessary for the deniers of hyle who say that the body is essentially only substantial connection. And it is not hidden from the fair that division, as it is not total annihilation, is also not annihilation with respect to some parts, for the intellect judges that water after division is the previous water, except that unity has left it and multiplicity has occurred to it. His statement: 'without its individual identity' Based on the view that unity is not among the individualizers; and the philosopher says that the hyle of the elements remain with individualization despite their multiplicity by consideration of elemental bodies. His statement: (And the universal things) i.e., concepts described with universality in themselves are described with unity without individuation, since their individuation occurs after the occurrence of individualizers.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a proof that unity (al-wahda) is not identical to existence (al-wujud). The commentator first quotes the original text which presents the view that some think unity is the same as existence, so individual unity is individual existence. Then the original text provides a refutation: if existence were identical to unity, then dividing one body (like splitting a sea) would annihilate the original and create two new bodies, which is absurd. The one who permits this is unreasonable. The original text adds that those who permit it rely on the idea that corporeal form is a continuous identity, and division ends that identity, but the underlying hyle remains. Then the commentator gives detailed explanations of each part: he clarifies that unity is accidental to multiplicity, he explains why the refutation holds, he shows that the absurdity is clear to reason, and he discusses the role of hyle, arguing that the hyle's unity is preserved by the specific unity of the form, not by individual unity. He concludes that division is not annihilation of the whole or of parts, as water after division is the same water except unity is lost.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الوجود
التفريق
الإعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and existence are the same because they always coexist.
Correction: The argument shows that coexistence is not identity; division proves distinctness.

Checkpoint
What is the core assumption that the refutation targets?
A) Unity and existence are accidental.
B) Unity is the same as existence.
C) Division does not affect unity.
D) The ocean is a good example.

Key Takeaway
Unity is not identical to existence; otherwise, division would entail annihilation, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you felt that a change in your identity (a broken relationship, a lost role) threatened your very existence? How does this lesson's distinction between unity and existence free you from that fear?
?
The author says that anyone who denies the obvious is not worth debating. Is there an area of your life where you stubbornly deny a clear truth about yourself? What would it take for you to stop 'contravening your own intellect'?
?
If you truly believe your existence is not defined by your unity or wholeness, how would that change the way you handle failure or fragmentation in your life? What concrete step can you take today to live out this truth?

Hyle and Corporeal Form: Defending the Argument
Rebuttal to the hyle-based objection to the division argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector's appeal to hyle and corporeal form is addressed, and why the refutation still stands.

Core Idea
Even if corporeal form changes, the persisting hyle does not salvage the unity=existence thesis.

The previous argument assumed that division would annihilate the original. An objector says that if the body's identity is in its continuous form, division creates new forms while hyle remains. This lesson explains why that does not undermine the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's view
Read the passage describing the objector's position: corporeal form is a continuous identity; division breaks it, but hyle persists.
This view is attributed to philosophers who believe in hyle.
2
See the author's dismissal
The author dismisses the objector as a 'contravener of the requirement of his intellect' and says such people are not to be addressed.
The dismissal is based on the clear rational judgment that division is not annihilation.
3
Understand the commentator's elaboration
The commentator explains that even on the hyle view, the individual unity of the hyle is not the same as the unity of the composite body. The division does not annihilate the hyle, but it does annihilate the specific unity of the body.
The commentator uses the example of water after division: it remains water, only no longer one.
4
Conclude the argument
The original proof remains valid because the unity that was claimed to be identical to existence is the specific unity of the body, not the underlying hyle's unity.
Thus, the refutation stands: if unity and existence were identical, division would still be annihilation of that specific existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Fourth Observatory - First Objective: Unity is concomitant with existence for one thing from two aspects. And we may say: Unity is an accident of multiplicity essentially, and of the many accidentally. And because of the equality between them, some thought that it (i.e., unity) is identical to existence, so that individual unity is the individual existence established for every determinate existent. And it is refuted by: that if existence (individual) were identical to unity (individual), then division (occurring in one body) would be annihilation (of that individualized body) and bringing into existence of two other bodies, because by division the specific unity is annulled, so the specific existence is annulled. And that (i.e., that division is annihilation) is false, since the splitting of the ocean with a mosquito's needle is not annihilation of it and bringing into existence of two other oceans, necessarily. Commentary
This passage is a proof that unity (al-wahda) is not identical to existence (al-wujud). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If hyle persists, then the original body still exists, so unity and existence might still be identical.
Correction: The unity that was claimed to be identical to existence is the unity of the composite, not the hyle. Division destroys that composite unity.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the water after division?
A) It is a new water.
B) It is the same water but now many.
C) It is annihilated and recreated.
D) It only exists in the hyle.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of hyle does not save the claim that unity=existence, because the unity at stake is that of the composite, not the substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of yourself as the same person you were years ago, despite many changes. But what if your core identity is like the hyle—merely a substrate—while your specific 'form' has been replaced many times? How would you know if you are still the same 'you'? What does this lesson demand you reconsider about the basis of your own selfhood?
?
The water example shows that what you call 'the same water' is actually many after division. In your life, can you point to any relationship, habit, or belief that you thought was a single continuous thing, but upon reflection is actually many fragments? Will you admit that your perceived unity may be an illusion, and how does that change your responsibility toward each fragment?
?
If the hyle's unity depends entirely on the form, then what is your own 'form'—the thing that holds your many experiences and roles into one person? Have you ever tried to define it precisely, or do you just assume it's there? What would it mean for you if that form could be lost while you still exist? How would you prepare for that possibility?

Existence vs. Unity: The Multiplicity Argument
Commentary explaining the proof that existence differs from unity regarding multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence can accompany multiplicity while unity cannot, and the accidental nature of attributing unity to the many.

Core Idea
Existence can be shared by many things; oneness cannot.

This lesson unpacks a key argument from classical kalam: that existence differs from unity in its relation to multiplicity. The commentator clarifies how we can speak of 'the many as one' without contradiction, showing that the oneness of a multitude is accidental. This distinction supports the broader proof for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice that the passage is a commentary on a prior statement, explaining the logical relationship between existence and unity.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the core point is clear.
2
Identify the main claim
The argument states that existence can occur without unity, showing they are not necessarily concomitant. In contrast, unity cannot be applied to something that is inherently many without a special consideration.
3
Understand the nuance of 'the many is one'
The commentator explains that when we say 'the many is one', we mean that the many is described as one when considered with its quality of multiplicity. The unity attaches accidentally, not essentially.
This resolves an apparent contradiction.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal importance
This distinction is crucial for proving that divine attributes like existence and unity are not simple synonyms, and that the multiple names for God refer to distinct realities.
It also safeguards against pantheism (identifying existence with unity).

Second Proof: Unity Is Not Individuation (Tashakhkhush)
Proof that unity is not individuation via division and universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second proof argues that unity is not identical to individuation, using the nature of universals and addressing objections about mental existence.

Core Idea
Unity describes universal concepts, not individuated particulars.

The commentator continues the discussion from the previous proof, applying the same logic to show that unity cannot be the same as individuation. He uses the example of universals, which are one in concept but become many only through individualizers. This lesson unpacks the argument, the refutation of a mental-existence objection, and an unresolved objection about identity to unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow. Notice the commentator's critical tone at the start.
The passage begins with the commentator noting that the proof is not fully satisfactory.
2
Understand the universal argument
Identify the claim: universals (like 'humanity') are described with unity but not individuation. This is the key evidence.
Universals are one in essence, but are not individuated until they receive individualizers.
3
Examine the mental-existence objection
The objection says mental existents are particular images. The refutation says they are particular in existence but universal in concept.
This shows that unity belongs to their concept, not to their mental existence.
4
Consider the deeper objection
The deeper objection asks: why can't individuation be identical to unity in some cases? The commentator begins to answer using the analogy of existence and God's essence.
The passage cuts off, but the idea is that identity in one case does not imply general identity.

Unity as an Additional Attribute
Establishes that unity is not identical to essence but an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that unity (al-wahda) is not identical to essence but an additional attribute, based on the essence's capacity to accept multiplicity.

Core Idea
Unity is not the same as essence, because essence accepts multiplicity while unity repels it.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful argument from the tradition of kalam. The author establishes that unity (al-wahda) is not identical to the essence but is an attribute added to it. The proof hinges on a key observation: essence by itself can accept multiplicity, but when unity is added, it rejects multiplicity. This shows that unity is neither the essence nor a part of it, following the same reasoning pattern used for existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's wording and structure sink in.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage opens by stating that unity is concomitant with existence. Then it immediately provides a proof that unity is different from essence and additional to it. Locate this claim.
The claim is at the beginning of the second sentence.
3
Understand the proof
The proof works by considering what would happen if unity and essence were identical. If they were identical, whenever one applies to something from an aspect, the other would also apply from the same aspect. But this is not the case because essence accepts multiplicity while unity rejects it.
The key is the contrast between essence's receptivity to multiplicity and unity's refusal of it.
4
Connect to the analogy
The passage ends by saying this reasoning follows the pattern used earlier for existence. This links the argument to a broader philosophical framework.
If you have studied the discussion of existence, recall how that argument worked.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if unity were identical to essence, it would not be a distinct attribute. But the proof shows it is distinct, which has implications for understanding divine attributes.
This argument reinforces that attributes are not identical to the essence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : الوحدة تساوق الوجود. وذلك دليل التغاير إذ لو كانا متحدين لكان إذا صدق أحدهما على شيء من جهة صدق عليه الآخر من تلك الجهة. وهي أي الوحدة مغايرة للماهية زائدة عليها؛ لأنها أي الماهية من حيث هي تقبل الكثرة، وإذا أخذت مع الوحدة تأباها، فلا تكون الوحدة نفسها ولا جزاها على قياس ما مر في بحث الوجود.
The Fourth Observatory - First Objective: Unity is concomitant with existence. And that is the proof of difference, for if they were identical, then when one of them applies to something from an aspect, the other would apply to it from that same aspect. And it (i.e., unity) is different from the essence and additional to it, because the essence (i.e., the quiddity), in itself, accepts multiplicity, and when taken with unity, it refuses it; so unity is neither the essence itself nor a part of it, analogous to what preceded in the discussion of existence.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage proves that unity is not identical to essence but is an additional attribute. It begins by stating the topic: unity is concomitant with existence. Then it provides a proof of distinction: if unity and essence were identical, then whenever one applies to something, the other would also apply in the same respect. But that is not the case, because essence by itself is receptive to multiplicity, whereas when combined with unity, it rejects multiplicity. Therefore, unity is neither the essence itself nor a part of it. This reasoning follows the same pattern as earlier arguments concerning existence.

Multiplicity is Additional to Essence
Sanusi argues that multiplicity is additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that multiplicity is not identical to essence but an additional attribute, based on the reasoning that essence is receptive to unity while multiplicity implies multiplicity.

Core Idea
Multiplicity is not the same as essence; it is added to it.

This lesson examines the first part of the argument that multiplicity is distinct from essence. The author uses an analogy with humanity to show that essence, when considered in itself, is receptive to unity, but when combined with multiplicity, it resists it, indicating that multiplicity is an extra feature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the claim.
2
Identify the claim
Note that the author asserts multiplicity is not essence but additional to it.
3
Understand the analogy
Consider the example of humanity: essence can be one or many, but when taken explicitly with multiplicity, it rejects it. This shows they are separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكثرة أيضاً غير الماهية، بل زائدة عليها لمثل ذلك؛ فإن الماهية كالإنسانية مثلاً من حيث هي قابلة للوحدة إذا أخذت مع الكثرة مفصلة كانت آبية عنها. والكثرة غير الوجود، وإلا يلزم كون الجمع إعداماً فإنه إذا جمع أجسام كميات في ظروف متعددة، وجعلت في ظرف واحد، فقد زالت كثرتها التي هي وجودها فرضاً، فيلزم إعدام تلك الأجسام وإيجاد جسم واحد وأنه باطل والمجوز مكابر.
And multiplicity is also not the essence, rather it is additional to it for a similar reason; for the essence—e.g., humanity—insofar as it is receptive to unity, when taken with multiplicity explicitly, it is averse to it. And multiplicity is not existence, for otherwise combination would be annihilation: for if one gathers multiple quantified bodies in different containers and places them in a single container, then their multiplicity, which is assumed to be their existence, would have been removed; thus it would necessitate the annihilation of those bodies and the creation of a single body, which is false, and whoever permits it is obstinate.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage continues the argument regarding multiplicity. First, it asserts that multiplicity is not the same as essence, using an analogous argument: essence (e.g., humanity) is receptive to unity, but when taken with multiplicity explicitly, it rejects it—showing they are distinct. Second, it argues that multiplicity is not identical to existence. The proof is a reductio: if multiplicity were existence, then combining multiple bodies into one container would eliminate their multiplicity (their existence), resulting in the annihilation of the bodies and the creation of a new single body, which is absurd. Therefore, multiplicity must be distinct from existence.

Multiplicity is not Existence
Sanusi proves that multiplicity is not existence through a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that multiplicity is distinct from existence, proven by a reductio argument involving the combination of bodies.

Core Idea
If multiplicity were existence, combining bodies would destroy them.

This lesson explores the second part of the argument, which proves that multiplicity is not identical to existence. The author presents a hypothetical scenario where multiple bodies in separate containers are combined into one; if multiplicity were their existence, they would be annihilated—an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to follow the argument.
2
Understand the reductio assumption
Note that the argument assumes, for the sake of contradiction, that multiplicity is identical to existence.
3
Analyze the scenario
Imagine multiple bodies in separate containers placed into one container. Their multiplicity (assumed existence) is removed, implying annihilation. Since bodies are not annihilated, the assumption is false.

The Debate's Starting Point: What Does 'Existence' Mean?
Commentator clarifies the meaning of 'existence' and addresses the role of universality/particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'their existence' in the debate about unity and multiplicity, and why universality and particularity are irrelevant to this issue.

Core Idea
The debate is about individuals, not concepts — and universality doesn't matter.

Before diving into the arguments for and against the external existence of unity and multiplicity, the commentator clarifies the precise scope of the discussion. He explains that the phrase 'their existence' refers to the existence of individual instances of unity and multiplicity in external reality, and he addresses a potential confusion about whether these are taken as universals or particulars. This groundwork is essential for evaluating the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the first clarification about 'their existence'.
Read the opening lines explaining that 'their existence' means the existence of individuals in external reality. Note that none of these individuals exist in the soul.
The phrase 'their existence' is from the main text being commented upon.
2
Understand the objection about particularity and its answer.
Read the objection that if the particular is impressed in the soul, the accident's particularity would necessitate the subject's particularity. Then read the answer: for non-material things, what occurs in the soul is unity and universal considerations, not personal essences. Also note the second objection about universals in instruments and its answer.
The key point is that universality and particularity have no role; the criterion is the aspect of perception.
3
Note the contradiction about philosophers and its resolution.
Read the commentator's observation that the claim 'the philosophers affirmed them' contradicts another statement that the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent. Then note his resolution: only some philosophers affirmed, others denied.
This sets the stage for the two opposing sides.

قوله : ( في وجودهما ) أي في وجود أفرادهما في الخارج بمعنى أن بعض أفرادهما موجودة في الخارج وهي القائمة بالموجودات الخارجية إذ لا شيء من أفرادهما بموجودة فيه، بل هي

قوله : (فأثبته الحكماء) يناقضه ما سيصرح به من أن تعريف الحكماء لا ينتقض بالوحدة؛ لأنها عدمية والظاهر أن المثبتين بعض الحكماء والنافين بعضهم، وهم الذين قالوا :
أنت فيما مر ( على المأخذ من الجانبين فيقال من جانب المثبت : الوحدة جزء من الواحد الموجود في الخارج، فتكون موجودة فيه، وأيضاً : لو كانت عدمية لم تتحقق إلا باعتبار العقل، فلا يكون الواحد واحداً في نفسه. وأيضاً هي نقيض اللاوحدة العدمية، وأيضاً لا فرق بين وحدته وبين لا وحدته له، وقد عرفت أجوبتها أيضاً، وقس حال الكثرة عليها. ويقال من جانب النافي : لو وجدت الوحدة لشاركت الوحدات في الوحدة، وامتازت عنها بخصوصية فللوحدة وحدة أخرى، وأيضاً لو كانت
،

:
:قوله على المأخوذ من الجانبين فمأخذ الدليل الأول للمثبت مر في بحث التعين، ومأخذ الأدلة الباقية الإمكان مر في بحث .
قوله : ( لو وجدت الوحدة أي وحدة من الوحدات لشاركت سائر الوحدات في حقيقة الوحدة المطلقة، وامتازت عنها بخصوصية ثبوتية يوجب تميزها عنها ضرورة أن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به ،الامتياز فيكون للكل وحدة، فوجوده غير الوحدة التي هي جزؤها وغير وحدة الخصوصية، لمغايرة وحدة الكل لوحدة الأجزاء، فيكون للوحدة وحدة أخرى مغايرة لها بالذات، وننقل الكلام إلى الوحدة الثانية بأنها مشاركة للوحدات في مطلق الوحدة، وممتازة عنها بخصوصية، فلوحدة الوحدة وحدة أخرى وهلم جراً. فيلزم التسلسل في الأمور الثابتة في نفس الأمر، المتغايرة بالذات بخلاف ما إذا كانت الوحدة عدمية، فإنها لا تتصف بالوحدة، فلا يلزم التسلسل هذا غاية تحرير هذا الدليل، لكنه يدل على رفع الإيجاب الكلي، لا على السلب الكلي، أعني لا شيء من الوحدات بموجودة؛ لجواز أن تكون وحدة الوحدة اعتبارية. وإنما يستدل الشارح قدس سره على نفي وجوديتها بأنها لو وجدت لكانت وحدة، لأنها تساوق الوجود، فللوحدة وحدة أخرى؛ لأن غرضه إيراد دليل اطلعت على مأخذه فيما مر. على أنه يرد عليه أنه يجوز أن يكون وحدة الوحدة نفسها.
إن كل عدد مؤلف مما تحته من الأعداد، وإلا لزم التسلسل المحال، كما سنذكره في بحث العلة والمعلول.
قوله : ( ويقال من جانب النافي إلخ الأظهر أن يقال : لو وجدت الوحدة لكانت واحدة؛ لكون الوجود مساوقاً للوحدة، فلها وحدة موجودة، وهلم جراً . قوله : (بخصوصية هي موجودة أيضاً، وكل موجود له وحدة، فللوحدة وحدة أخرى.
His statement: '(regarding their existence)' means regarding the existence of their individuals in external reality, in the sense that some of their individuals exist in external reality, namely those subsisting in external existents, since none of their individuals exist in it (i.e., the soul), but rather they are... His statement: '(whether taken as universals or particulars)' means whether the two accidents are taken [as universal or particular]. It was said: From the permissibility of the impression of the particular in the soul, it follows that the particularity of the accident necessitates the particularity of the subject. Unless one chooses that for non-material things apparently, although it is established in its place that what occurs in the soul itself without the mediation of the instruments from non-material particulars is unity and universal considerations, not their personal essences. Also, it would necessitate the permissibility of the impression of the universal in the instruments by the subsistence of the subject of universality in them, contrary to what is established with them. And it was answered: The intent is only that universality and particularity have no role in this matter; rather the criterion is the mentioned [aspect], not the actual permissibility of the two accidents being universal or particular. 
The aspect
His statement: '(so the philosophers affirmed them)' is contradicted by what he will clarify, namely that the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent, and apparently the affirmers are some philosophers and the deniers are some of them, and they are those who said:
You [already know] from what preceded concerning the basis from both sides. Thus it is said from the side of the affirmer: Unity is a part of the one existing externally, so it is existent in it. Also, if it were non-existent, it would not be realized except by a conceptual consideration, so the one would not be one in itself. Also, it is the contrary of non-unity, which is non-existent. Also, there would be no difference between its unity and its non-unity. You already know their answers as well, and apply the same to multiplicity. And it is said from the side of the denier: If unity existed, it would share with [other] unities in unity and be distinguished from them by a particularity, so unity would have another unity. Also, if they were... conceptual matters that the intellect extracts from existents, not in the existence of their essences, for that would be an impossible independence, and within individuals it is a branch of the problem of the existence of universals. The mentioned proofs indicate what we said. His statement: '(so the philosophers affirmed them)' meaning the ancients, hence they made number a category of quantity and added to the definition of quality the condition of indivisibility, while the later ones omitted this condition because unity is non-existent according to them, and they strained to make number a category of quantity on the assumption that it exists.
His statement: 'based on the two sides' — the basis of the first proof for the affirmer passed in the discussion of determination, and the basis of the remaining proofs passed in the discussion of possibility.
His statement: '(If unity existed)' meaning any one of the unities, it would share with the rest of the unities in the reality of absolute unity and be distinguished from them by a positive particularity that necessitates its differentiation from them, necessarily because that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is distinction. Thus the whole would have a unity, and its existence is other than the unity that is part of it and other than the unity of the particularity, because the unity of the whole is different from the unity of the parts. So unity would have another unity essentially different from it. Then we transfer the discussion to the second unity, that it shares with unities in absolute unity and is distinguished from them by a particularity, so the unity of unity would have yet another unity, and so on. This entails a regress in matters established in themselves, essentially different. This is contrary to the case if unity were non-existent, for then it would not be characterized by unity, so no regress follows. This is the fullest formulation of this proof. But it proves the negation of universal affirmation, not universal negation, i.e., that none of the unities exist, because it is possible that the unity of unity is conceptual. The commentator (may his secret be sanctified) only argues for the negation of its existence by [saying] that if it existed it would be a unity, because it coexists with existence, so unity would have another unity; because his purpose is to present a proof whose basis you have seen in what preceded. However, it may be objected that it is possible that the unity of unity is identical to itself. 
Every number is composed of what is under it among numbers, otherwise an impossible regress would follow, as we will mention in the discussion of cause and effect.
His statement: '(And it is said from the side of the denier, etc.)' — it is more apparent to say: If unity existed, it would be one; because existence coexists with unity, so it would have an existent unity, and so on. His statement: '(by a particularity that is also existent, and every existent has a unity, so unity would have another unity.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical discussion of whether unity and multiplicity have external existence. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'regarding their existence' from the main text, explaining that it refers to the existence of their individuals in external reality. He then addresses the phrase 'whether taken as universals or particulars,' answering an objection about the permissibility of the impression of the particular in the soul: the particularity of the accident does not necessitate the particularity of the subject for non-material things, and what occurs in the soul is unity and universal considerations, not personal essences. Another objection concerning the impression of the universal in the instruments is answered by stating that universality and particularity are irrelevant to this matter; the criterion is the aspect of perception, not actual universality or particularity. The commentator then notes a contradiction: the claim that philosophers affirmed the external existence of unity and multiplicity conflicts with another passage where the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent. He resolves this by suggesting that only some philosophers affirmed and some denied. He then presents arguments from both sides. From the side of the affirmer: unity is a part of the one, so it exists; if it were non-existent, the one would not be one in itself; it is the contrary of non-unity; and if non-existent, there would be no difference between unity and its absence. From the side of the denier: if unity existed, each instance would share in absolute unity and be distinguished by a particularity, so the whole would have a unity, and that unity would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress. The commentator elaborates this regress argument in detail, showing that if unity existed, each unit would have a unity distinct from itself, leading to a chain. He then notes that this argument only negates universal affirmation, not universal denial — it allows for the possibility that the unity of unity is merely conceptual. The commentator's own argument for negation is that if unity existed, it would be one (since existence coexists with unity), so it would have another unity. However, he acknowledges an objection: the unity of unity might be identical to itself. The passage concludes with a note on number and a restatement of the denier's argument that if unity existed, it would be one and thus have another unity. This entire passage illustrates the complexity of the debate over the ontological status of unity and multiplicity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the debate concerns the universal concepts of unity and multiplicity, not their instances.
Correction: The commentator specifies that the debate is about the existence of individual instances of unity and multiplicity in external reality, not about the universal concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'their existence' refer to in the debate about unity and multiplicity?
A) The existence of the universal concepts of unity and multiplicity.
B) The existence of individual instances of unity and multiplicity in external reality.
C) The existence of unity and multiplicity in the mind.
D) The existence of the accidents of unity and multiplicity in the soul.

Key Takeaway
The debate about unity and multiplicity concerns the external existence of their individual instances, not universal concepts, and universality/particularity are irrelevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often confuse intellectual concepts with the actual reality they represent? In your worship, are you engaging with the reality of God's oneness or just a mental concept? How can you bridge that gap today?
?
The commentator emphasizes that universality is irrelevant. Think of an attribute of God you struggle with. Are you approaching it as a universal notion or as a particular reality that demands your response? What changes if you treat it as real?
?
This lesson challenges you to distinguish between mental constructs and external existence. In your life, are there beliefs or practices that are mere mental constructs without affecting your external actions? What concrete step can you take to align your external existence with your internal conviction?

The Affirmers' Case: Four Proofs for Unity's Existence
Four short arguments from the affirmer side supporting the external existence of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the four arguments presented by those who affirm that unity exists externally, and understand the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Affirmers argue from part-whole, actuality, contrariety, and indistinguishability.

Having set the stage, the commentator now presents the arguments from the side of those who affirm the external existence of unity. These four succinct proofs draw on different logical and metaphysical principles. Reading them carefully reveals the core reasoning of the affirmers, even as the commentator notes that their answers are already known.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument: unity as a part of the one.
The first argument states that unity is a part of the one existing externally, so it must itself exist externally. Reflect on the part-whole relationship.
This is a direct ontological argument.
2
Read the second argument: unity cannot be non-existent.
If unity were non-existent, it would only be realized conceptually, and then the one would not be truly one in itself. Consider the implication for objective unity.
3
Read the third argument: unity is the contrary of non-unity.
Since non-unity is non-existent, its contrary (unity) must be existent. This uses the principle of contraries.
4
Read the fourth argument: no difference between unity and its absence.
If unity were non-existent, there would be no difference between a thing's unity and its lack of unity. This argues from the need for a real distinction.
The commentator says the answers to these are known elsewhere.

The commentator (may his secret be sanctified) only argues for the negation of its existence by [saying] that if it existed it would be a unity, because it coexists with existence, so unity would have another unity; because his purpose is to present a proof whose basis you have seen in what preceded. However, it may be objected that it is possible that the unity of unity is identical to itself. 
Every number is composed of what is under it among numbers, otherwise an impossible regress would follow, as we will mention in the discussion of cause and effect.
His statement: '(And it is said from the side of the denier, etc.)' — it is more apparent to say: If unity existed, it would be one; because existence coexists with unity, so it would have an existent unity, and so on. His statement: '(by a particularity that is also existent, and every existent has a unity, so unity would have another unity.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical discussion of whether unity and multiplicity have external existence. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'regarding their existence' from the main text, explaining that it refers to the existence of their individuals in external reality. He then addresses the phrase 'whether taken as universals or particulars,' answering an objection about the permissibility of the impression of the particular in the soul: the particularity of the accident does not necessitate the particularity of the subject for non-material things, and what occurs in the soul is unity and universal considerations, not personal essences. Another objection concerning the impression of the universal in the instruments is answered by stating that universality and particularity are irrelevant to this matter; the criterion is the aspect of perception, not actual universality or particularity. The commentator then notes a contradiction: the claim that philosophers affirmed the external existence of unity and multiplicity conflicts with another passage where the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent. He resolves this by suggesting that only some philosophers affirmed and some denied. He then presents arguments from both sides. From the side of the affirmer: unity is a part of the one, so it exists; if it were non-existent, the one would not be one in itself; it is the contrary of non-unity; and if non-existent, there would be no difference between unity and its absence. From the side of the denier: if unity existed, each instance would share in absolute unity and be distinguished by a particularity, so the whole would have a unity, and that unity would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress. The commentator elaborates this regress argument in detail, showing that if unity existed, each unit would have a unity distinct from itself, leading to a chain. He then notes that this argument only negates universal affirmation, not universal denial — it allows for the possibility that the unity of unity is merely conceptual. The commentator's own argument for negation is that if unity existed, it would be one (since existence coexists with unity), so it would have another unity. However, he acknowledges an objection: the unity of unity might be identical to itself. The passage concludes with a note on number and a restatement of the denier's argument that if unity existed, it would be one and thus have another unity. This entire passage illustrates the complexity of the debate over the ontological status of unity and multiplicity.

The Deniers' Regress: Why Unity Cannot Exist Externally
Detailed presentation and analysis of the deniers' regress argument against the external existence of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the regress argument against the external existence of unity, its scope, and the commentator's own version and objection.

Core Idea
If unity existed externally, each instance would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress.

The deniers' central argument is a powerful regress: if unity existed as a positive entity, then each instance of unity would share in a common nature and be distinguished by a particularity, necessitating a further unity for the composite. This leads to an endless chain. The commentator elaborates this argument, clarifies that it only negates universal affirmation, and then presents his own version along with a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial regress argument from the denier.
Read the first statement: if unity existed, it would share with other unities and differ by a particularity, so the whole would have a unity, leading to another unity. Grasp the core idea.
2
Follow the detailed elaboration of the regress.
The commentator expands: each unity shares in absolute unity and has a distinctive particularity. The unity of the whole is different from the unity of parts, so the whole's unity requires another unity, and so on. This yields an essentially infinite regress.
Note that the regress is in 'matters established in themselves, essentially different'.
3
Understand the scope: it negates universal affirmation, not universal negation.
The commentator points out that this proof only shows that not all unities can be existent (i.e., it refutes the claim that every unity exists), but it does not prove that no unity exists, because the unity of unity could be merely conceptual.
This is a crucial subtlety.
4
Examine the commentator's own version and the objection.
The commentator argues more simply: if unity existed, it would be one (since existence coexists with unity), so it would have another unity. He then notes an objection: the unity of unity might be identical to itself, avoiding the regress.

His statement: '(regarding their existence)' means regarding the existence of their individuals in external reality, in the sense that some of their individuals exist in external reality, namely those subsisting in external existents, since none of their individuals exist in it (i.e., the soul), but rather they are... His statement: '(whether taken as universals or particulars)' means whether the two accidents are taken [as universal or particular]. It was said: From the permissibility of the impression of the particular in the soul, it follows that the particularity of the accident necessitates the particularity of the subject. Unless one chooses that for non-material things apparently, although it is established in its place that what occurs in the soul itself without the mediation of the instruments from non-material particulars is unity and universal considerations, not their personal essences. Also, it would necessitate the permissibility of the impression of the universal in the instruments by the subsistence of the subject of universality in them, contrary to what is established with them. And it was answered: The intent is only that universality and particularity have no role in this matter; rather the criterion is the mentioned [aspect], not the actual permissibility of the two accidents being universal or particular. 
The aspect
His statement: '(so the philosophers affirmed them)' is contradicted by what he will clarify, namely that the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent, and apparently the affirmers are some philosophers and the deniers are some of them, and they are those who said:
You [already know] from what preceded concerning the basis from both sides. Thus it is said from the side of the affirmer: Unity is a part of the one existing externally, so it is existent in it. Also, if it were non-existent, it would not be realized except by a conceptual consideration, so the one would not be one in itself. Also, it is the contrary of non-unity, which is non-existent. Also, there would be no difference between its unity and its non-unity. You already know their answers as well, and apply the same to multiplicity. And it is said from the side of the denier: If unity existed, it would share with [other] unities in unity and be distinguished from them by a particularity, so unity would have another unity. Also, if they were... conceptual matters that the intellect extracts from existents, not in the existence of their essences, for that would be an impossible independence, and within individuals it is a branch of the problem of the existence of universals. The mentioned proofs indicate what we said. His statement: '(so the philosophers affirmed them)' meaning the ancients, hence they made number a category of quantity and added to the definition of quality the condition of indivisibility, while the later ones omitted this condition because unity is non-existent according to them, and they strained to make number a category of quantity on the assumption that it exists.
His statement: 'based on the two sides' — the basis of the first proof for the affirmer passed in the discussion of determination, and the basis of the remaining proofs passed in the discussion of possibility.
His statement: '(If unity existed)' meaning any one of the unities, it would share with the rest of the unities in the reality of absolute unity and be distinguished from them by a positive particularity that necessitates its differentiation from them, necessarily because that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is distinction. Thus the whole would have a unity, and its existence is other than the unity that is part of it and other than the unity of the particularity, because the unity of the whole is different from the unity of the parts. So unity would have another unity essentially different from it. Then we transfer the discussion to the second unity, that it shares with unities in absolute unity and is distinguished from them by a particularity, so the unity of unity would have yet another unity, and so on. This entails a regress in matters established in themselves, essentially different. This is contrary to the case if unity were non-existent, for then it would not be characterized by unity, so no regress follows. This is the fullest formulation of this proof. But it proves the negation of universal affirmation, not universal negation, i.e., that none of the unities exist, because it is possible that the unity of unity is conceptual. The commentator (may his secret be sanctified) only argues for the negation of its existence by [saying] that if it existed it would be a unity, because it coexists with existence, so unity would have another unity; because his purpose is to present a proof whose basis you have seen in what preceded. However, it may be objected that it is possible that the unity of unity is identical to itself. 
Every number is composed of what is under it among numbers, otherwise an impossible regress would follow, as we will mention in the discussion of cause and effect.
His statement: '(And it is said from the side of the denier, etc.)' — it is more apparent to say: If unity existed, it would be one; because existence coexists with unity, so it would have an existent unity, and so on. His statement: '(by a particularity that is also existent, and every existent has a unity, so unity would have another unity.'

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical discussion of whether unity and multiplicity have external existence. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'regarding their existence' from the main text, explaining that it refers to the existence of their individuals in external reality. He then addresses the phrase 'whether taken as universals or particulars,' answering an objection about the permissibility of the impression of the particular in the soul: the particularity of the accident does not necessitate the particularity of the subject for non-material things, and what occurs in the soul is unity and universal considerations, not personal essences. Another objection concerning the impression of the universal in the instruments is answered by stating that universality and particularity are irrelevant to this matter; the criterion is the aspect of perception, not actual universality or particularity. The commentator then notes a contradiction: the claim that philosophers affirmed the external existence of unity and multiplicity conflicts with another passage where the philosophers' definition is not invalidated by unity because it is non-existent. He resolves this by suggesting that only some philosophers affirmed and some denied. He then presents arguments from both sides. From the side of the affirmer: unity is a part of the one, so it exists; if it were non-existent, the one would not be one in itself; it is the contrary of non-unity; and if non-existent, there would be no difference between unity and its absence. From the side of the denier: if unity existed, each instance would share in absolute unity and be distinguished by a particularity, so the whole would have a unity, and that unity would require another unity, leading to an infinite regress. The commentator elaborates this regress argument in detail, showing that if unity existed, each unit would have a unity distinct from itself, leading to a chain. He then notes that this argument only negates universal affirmation, not universal denial — it allows for the possibility that the unity of unity is merely conceptual. The commentator's own argument for negation is that if unity existed, it would be one (since existence coexists with unity), so it would have another unity. However, he acknowledges an objection: the unity of unity might be identical to itself. The passage concludes with a note on number and a restatement of the denier's argument that if unity existed, it would be one and thus have another unity. This entire passage illustrates the complexity of the debate over the ontological status of unity and multiplicity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the regress argument proves that no unity exists at all (universal negation).
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it only proves that not every unity can exist (negation of universal affirmation), because the unity of unity could be merely conceptual.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator state about the scope of the regress argument?
A) It proves that no unity exists.
B) It proves that unity exists only in the mind.
C) It negates universal affirmation, not universal negation.
D) It proves that unity is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
The deniers' regress argument shows that if unity existed, an infinite regress of unities would follow, but it only refutes the claim that all unities are existent, not that none are.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers used a clever regress argument to deny the external existence of unity. But if unity is not a real attribute, what does that mean for the way you understand the Oneness of God (Tawheed)? Does the Qur'anic declaration that God is One require a separate attribute of unity, or is it simply that He is not multiple? How does this affect your reliance on rational arguments versus direct revelation when affirming God's attributes? Are you willing to accept that some aspects of Tawheed might be purely conceptual and not positive attributes?
?
This lesson shows that the regress argument only negates universal affirmation, not universal negation. In other words, it does not prove that no unity exists. Does this nuance change how you view the strength of philosophical arguments in theology? Are you tempted to reject all rational arguments because they seem too technical, or do you see value in understanding their precise scope and limits? How can you guard against overconfidence or underconfidence in rational theology?
?
The commentator presents a simpler version of the regress: 'if unity existed, it would be one, so it would require another unity.' Think about your own understanding of God's oneness. Do you ever fall into the trap of imagining God's unity as if it were a separate quality added to His essence? How can you purify your conception of Tawheed so that you affirm God's oneness without implicitly multiplying entities? What practical steps can you take today to deepen your conviction in the absolute oneness of Allah, beyond mere intellectual assent?

Four Arguments for the Existence of Unity
Presents four arguments from the muthbit side for the existence of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational arguments presented by the affirmers (muthbitun) to prove that unity (al-wahda) is an existent attribute in the external world.

Core Idea
Unity must be externally existent; otherwise, nothing would be truly one in itself.

The affirmers of real attributes (muthbitun) advanced several arguments to show that unity is not a mere mental construct but a genuine existent in external objects. This passage presents four such arguments, each showing the absurdity of considering unity as non-existent. As you read, notice how each argument builds on a different logical angle: part-whole, mental dependence, opposition, and indiscernibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow. Note that the passage lists four arguments and ends with a reference to known answers and a comparison with multiplicity.
The passage is concise; each argument is just a few words.
2
Identify each argument
Identify the four distinct arguments signaled by 'أيضاً' (also) and the final one with 'أيضاً' (also) before 'لا فرق'. List them in your own words.
Argument markers: فيقال (it is said), وأيضاً (also) appears three times.
3
Understand the logic of each argument
For each argument, ask: what premise does it rely on? What conclusion does it draw about unity? The commentary will help you unpack the reasoning.
The third argument uses the principle that opposites cannot both be non-existent.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Consider why the author mentions that 'you have already known their answers' and why he instructs to compare the case of multiplicity. What does this tell you about the dialectical context?
The passage is part of a larger debate; the answers were given earlier in the book.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can distinguish the fourth argument from the others. Then try to explain one argument to a friend.
The fourth argument is the one about no difference between unity and non-unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال من جانب المثبت : الوحدة جزء من الواحد الموجود في الخارج، فتكون موجودة فيه، وأيضاً : لو كانت عدمية لم تتحقق إلا باعتبار العقل، فلا يكون الواحد واحداً في نفسه. وأيضاً هي نقيض اللاوحدة العدمية، وأيضاً لا فرق بين وحدته وبين لا وحدته له، وقد عرفت أجوبتها أيضاً، وقس حال الكثرة عليها.
It is said from the side of the affirmer: Unity is a part of the one that exists in the external world, so it exists in it. Also: If it were non-existent, it would not be realized except by mental consideration, so the one would not be one in itself. Also: It is the opposite of non-unity which is non-existent. Also: There is no difference between its unity and its non-unity for it. And you have already known their answers as well, and compare the case of multiplicity to it.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments from the muthbit (affirmer) side for the existence of unity (al-wahda) as a real attribute in external objects. The first argument: Unity is a part of the one (al-wahid) that exists externally, so unity must also exist externally since a part of an existent is existent. The second: If unity were non-existent ('adamiyya), it would only be realized by mental consideration, but then the one would not be one in itself (i.e., its oneness would depend on the mind). The third: Unity is the opposite of non-unity, which is non-existent, so unity must be existent (since opposites are both either existent or non-existent). The fourth: There is no difference between a thing's unity and its non-unity if unity were non-existent, which is absurd. The passage notes that the answers to these arguments are already known from earlier discussions. Finally, it instructs to compare the case of multiplicity (al-kathra) to unity, implying similar reasoning applies.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الواحد
العدم
المثبت
الكثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That unity is merely a mental construct without any external reality.
Correction: The four arguments demonstrate that unity must be externally existent: (1) it is part of an existent, (2) otherwise the one would not be one in itself, (3) it is opposite of non-existence, (4) otherwise unity and non-unity would be indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the fourth argument given by the muthbit for the existence of unity?
A) Unity is part of the one existing externally.
B) If unity were non-existent, the one would not be one in itself.
C) Unity is the opposite of non-unity which is non-existent.
D) There is no difference between a thing's unity and its non-unity.

Key Takeaway
The affirmers prove unity is an externally existent attribute through four rational arguments: part-whole, mental dependence, opposition, and indiscernibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second argument states that if unity were merely mental, the one would not be one in itself. Reflect on your own sense of personal identity: Have you ever felt that your 'oneness' as a person is fragile or purely psychological? How does this argument challenge you to see your own existence as having a real unity that reflects the Creator's oneness? Commit to recognizing your inherent unity as a sign of Allah's attribute al-Wahid.
?
The fourth argument says there would be no difference between unity and non-unity if unity were not real. Apply this to your faith: If the oneness of God (tawhid) were only a concept in your mind, would there be any real difference between belief and disbelief in your actions? Today, resolve to make the reality of Allah's unity transform one concrete aspect of your daily life—whether in your worship, your speech, or your dealings with others.
?
The first argument treats unity as a part of the one. Consider the things you consider 'one' in your life—your family, your community, your purpose. Do you treat their unity as a mere label, or as a real, binding attribute that demands your commitment? This week, identify one relationship or goal whose unity you have neglected, and take a practical step to honor its reality.

Unity and Multiplicity Are Not Essentially Opposite
The author argues that unity and multiplicity are not opposites by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are not opposites by essence, and how the kalam argument distinguishes essential opposition from accidental opposition based on the subject.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites by essence; their apparent opposition depends on the subject.

In this passage, the author tackles a common misconception in kalam: that unity (al-wahdah) and multiplicity (al-kathrah) are opposites by their very nature. He argues that this is false, because essential opposition requires a single subject, which unity and multiplicity do not necessarily share. The argument refines the concept of opposition (taqabul) and clarifies the relationship between these two fundamental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The author states at the outset: 'There is no essential opposition between their two realities.' This is the thesis to be defended.
Notice that the author immediately distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition.
2
Understand the definition of essential opposition
The author explains that true essential opposition requires that the two things be related to a single subject, such that the occurrence of one prevents the occurrence of the other. Unity and multiplicity do not meet this condition because they can apply to the same subject in different respects.
Essential opposition is not just difference but mutual exclusion in one subject.
3
Examine the refutation of the opposing view
The author refutes those who claim unity and multiplicity are opposites by essence. He points out that the subject of unity is often part of the subject of multiplicity, so they are not truly opposite. He also dismisses the argument that they are far apart or that one opposite can be constituted by the other.
The author calls such opponents 'pretentious' for lacking proof.
4
Consider the example of water and hylomorphism
The author uses the example of water: water can be multiple (in many jugs) and unified (in one jug) while its underlying matter (hyle) remains the same. This shows that unity and multiplicity can inhere in the same subject without essential opposition.
This example refutes the view that they are individuators (mushakhkhiṣāt).
5
Reflect on the conclusion about the intellect's role
The author concludes that our certainty that unity and multiplicity are opposed is only due to the mind's habit of assuming different subjects. True essential opposition requires the intellect to relate both to one thing and judge mutual exclusion.
The lesson ends with an invitation to 'reflect' (taʼammal).

The Fourth Observation - Third Purpose: Unity and Multiplicity are not essential. Unity: there is no essential opposition between their two realities at all. This is the intent of the people in this context, not that there is essential or accidental opposition between the concepts of their definitions. And the statement that the opposition between multiplicity and the accidental unity, one of them supervening upon the other annulling it, is the opposition of contradiction, is false; because of what you have known of the lack of union in subject, and because the discussion is about their realities, not about their individuals. And the mentioned unity, I mean the accidental unity supervening upon the subject of multiplicity, is a part of a multiplicity composed of units each of which is accidental upon a specific subject of multiplicity, and annulling it, so this unity itself is not opposite to the essence of multiplicity. And among the pretentious is one who said: Unity and multiplicity are two opposites; for we do not require between two opposites extreme difference, while unity and multiplicity are very far apart, and we also do not require the impossibility of one opposite being constituted by the other, while unity [by its individual] except that it has departed from that multiplicity, and a real unity has occurred to it. And the conclusion: We do not concede that unity and multiplicity are among the individuators, such that with the removal of one and the advent of the other the existence of their subject would cease, nor do we allow that they can be consecutive accidents, as is the doctrine of Plato concerning continuity and discontinuity. And what the commentator mentioned is based on hylomorphism such that it would necessitate the annihilation of the corporeal form which is the substrate of multiplicity in the jugs, if those waters are placed in one jug, and the occurrence of a single continuous form in itself with no division at all. So it does not constitute a proof against those who deny them, among them the author. Also, what will come later only indicates that a single corporeal form in person cannot be a subject for unity and multiplicity, and does not constitute a proof that one thing in person cannot be a subject for them. Why may it not be that their subject is the matter of the water remaining identically in both states, and it has been described in one state with multiplicity truly, even if through the form, and in the other with unity also through it, and that is sufficient for their unity of locus? And what is said that matter is not in itself one nor multiple, its meaning is that being described with either is not a requirement of its essence, not that it is not truly described with either, for that is denied. Annulling multiplicity, not constitutive of it, and we also do not require personal unity in the subject of the two opposites. Then he claimed that we know with certainty that their two essences are definitely opposed, disregarding measurability and being measured, and it is also refuted because that certainty of ours is only due to the mind's immediate understanding that the subject of unity is a part of the subject of multiplicity, so that which is described by both is not one thing. And that is not their opposition; rather they are essentially opposite only when the intellect relates them to one thing and judges that the occurrence of one in it prevents the occurrence of the other. So reflect, and God is the giver of success. His saying: 'And we also do not require...' etc. You have known that relation to a single personal subject is necessary in opposites, and since the falsity of these claims is known from what preceded, and the speaker did not prove them, the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) counted him among the pretentious, and did not undertake to explain their falsity. His saying: 'And it is also refuted...' etc. Its gist is that what is known necessarily is that one thing is not described by both, and that does not necessitate their opposition.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument refuting the claim that unity (al-wahdah) and multiplicity (al-kathrah) are opposites by essence. The author begins by stating the correct position: there is no essential opposition between their realities. The intent is not to claim opposition between the concepts of their definitions, but to show that the reality of each is not essentially opposed to the other. The author then refutes the view that the opposition between accidental unity and multiplicity is contradiction, because they do not share a single subject, and the discussion is about their essences, not their instances. The accidental unity that supervenes on the subject of multiplicity is part of a composite multiplicity and thus not opposite to the essence of multiplicity. The author then considers an objection from 'the pretentious' who claim that unity and multiplicity are true opposites, arguing that they are far apart and that one opposite can be constituted by the other. The author responds that the constitution of one opposite by the other is impossible, and that the appearance of opposition is due to the mind's improper attribution to a single subject. He then presents the core of his argument: unity and multiplicity are not individuators; their subject can remain the same even when one replaces the other, as in the example of water (hyle) that can be multiple in one state and unified in another without ceasing to exist. He refutes the claim that matter cannot be truly described by either unity or multiplicity, explaining that such descriptions are not essential but accidental. Finally, he refutes the claim that we know with certainty that the two essences are opposed, showing that our certainty is only due to the mental habit of assuming that the subject of unity is part of the subject of multiplicity, not to any real opposition. True essential opposition requires that the intellect relates both to one thing and judges that the occurrence of one prevents the occurrence of the other. The commentary explains that the commentator considered the objector among the pretentious for lacking proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are essentially opposite like black and white.
Correction: They are not essentially opposite; they can inhere in the same subject without mutual exclusion, and essential opposition requires a single subject.

Checkpoint
What is the core reason the author gives for why unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite?
A) Because they are both accidental attributes.
B) Because essential opposition requires a single subject, and unity and multiplicity do not necessarily share one.
C) Because they are indivisible and cannot be compared.
D) Because they are defined differently by different philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite; true essential opposition requires that the intellect relate both to one thing and judge mutual exclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you assumed two things in your deen — like fear and hope, or justice and mercy — were opposites because they seemed far apart. How does this lesson’s insistence that true opposition requires a single subject and mutual exclusion challenge you to re-examine that assumption? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you saw them as compatible rather than opposed?
?
The author says our certainty that unity and multiplicity are opposed is due to mental habit, not rational necessity. Where in your own understanding of Tawhid have you been content with a habitual certainty instead of a deep, reasoned conviction? What step will you take this week to replace habit with grounded belief?
?
If unity and multiplicity can coexist in the same matter without opposition, what does that teach you about the nature of Allah’s attributes? Are there attributes of Allah (like His mercy and wrath) that you have unconsciously treated as opposites? How does this lesson move you to praise Him with all His names without inner conflict?

The Ash'ari Linguistic Argument on Attributes and Essence
The Ash'ari linguistic argument on attribute-essence identity and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari linguistic argument for the identity of attributes with the essence and the criticism of its generalization.

Core Idea
Attributes are not 'other' than the essence: a linguistic test.

The Ash'ari theologians proposed a simple linguistic test to argue that attributes are not separate from the essence. However, this argument has been challenged and refined. This lesson examines the initial argument and the debate about its scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument.
Focus on the linguistic example.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice the Ash'ari claim that the attribute is not 'other' (ghayr) than the essence.
3
Understand the rebuttal
See how the reply distinguishes between 'other' meaning a separate entity versus a different description.
4
Recognize the generalization
The commentator infers that the Ash'aris hold this for all attributes, both necessary and accidental.
This generalization is contested later.
