Objection from Death and Life: The Real Ground of Opposition
Objection and response regarding the opposition of knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of coexistence does not necessarily imply opposition, and how the Ash'ari response distinguishes between opposition of essences and opposition of rulings.

Core Idea
Coexistence failure does not equal opposition; rulings can be the real barrier.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated objection to the Ash'ari doctrine that knowledge and ignorance are opposites. The objector uses the example of death and life—which the Ash'aris do not consider opposites—to argue that simple impossibility of coexistence is not sufficient for opposition. The response clarifies that the real ground for opposition lies in the impossibility of combining their rulings, not just their essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the objector argues that death and life are not considered opposites by the Ash'aris, yet they cannot coexist. This challenges the assumption that knowledge and ignorance must be opposites just because they cannot coexist.
Notice the objector is using the Ash'aris' own view on death and life against them.
2
Identify the response
The commentator explains that the impossibility of coexistence for knowledge and ignorance is not due to their essences but due to the impossibility of combining their rulings (ḥukmayhimā). This is the key distinction.
The term 'rulings' here refers to the effects or legal/ontological consequences.
3
Reflect on the nuance
Understand that opposition can have different bases. By grounding opposition in rulings rather than essences, the Ash'ari position remains coherent even if death and life are not considered opposites.
This protects the doctrine from the objection without requiring death to be an opposite of life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( يرد عليهم الموت والحياة على تقدير وجودية الموت كما يدل عليه ظاهر قوله تعالى : خلق الموت والحياة ) وحاصله أنا لا نسلم أن بين العلم والجهل المذكورين تضاداً، فإن امتناع اجتماعهما لا يستلزم التضاد كما في الموت والحياة عندكم، فالإيراد المذكور منع وسند. وليس بنقض ما يوهمه قوله ويرد عليهم الموت والحياة. قوله : (قال صاحب القنية إلخ) لما لم يثبت أن القائل بعدم التضاد بين الحياة والموت، وبأنه وجودي واحد بل إنما ثبت القولان منهم فلعل القائل متعدد كما هو الظاهر، إذ القول بعدم التضاد بينهما مع وجودية الموت مستبعد جداً. نقل الشارح قدس سره كلام صاحب القنية وأنه أورد الاعتراض بالموت والعلم. قوله : ( مع أنهما ليسا بضدين عندكم لعدم استحالة اجتماعهما لذاتيهما، لكن لا يخفى أنه لا فائدة حينئذ بالتقييد بقوله : عندكم . قوله: (وليس) المانع من ذلك تضادهما لأن استحالة اجتماعهما ليس لذاتيهما، بل لامتناع اجتماع حكميهما بينهما. قوله : ( يرد عليهم الموت والحياة) إذا ثبت كون الموت وجودياً، وعدم قولهم بالتضاد قوله : (قال صاحب القنية إلخ) قيل : كان الشارح استبعد عدم جعل الموت ضداً للحياة على تقدير وجوديته، فنقل كلام القنية إشارة إلى احتمال خلل في النقل من المصنف، فإن كلامه في العلم والموت لا في الموت والحياة لكنه يندفع عنه باعتبار قيد لذاتيهما في تعريف الضدين؛ إذ ليس عدم اجتماع الموت والعلم لذاتيهما، وكان المصنف غير كلامه لذلك، والحق أن ما ذكره المصنف مأخوذ من أبكار الأفكار فإن الاعتراض هناك بالموت والحياة.
His statement: (The objection is raised against them regarding death and life on the assumption that death is existent, as indicated by the apparent meaning of His saying: 'He created death and life') and the gist of it is that we do not concede that there is opposition between the mentioned knowledge and ignorance, for the impossibility of their coexistence does not necessitate opposition, as in the case of death and life according to you. So the mentioned objection is a prevention and a support. And it is not a refutation of what is suggested by his statement 'and the objection is raised against them regarding death and life.' His statement: (The author of al-Quniyyah said etc.) Since it has not been established that the one who claims the absence of opposition between life and death, and that it is one existent, rather only the two claims from them are established, so perhaps the speaker is multiple as is apparent, for the claim of no opposition between them while death is existent is very far-fetched. The commentator (may his secret be sanctified) transmitted the words of the author of al-Quniyyah and that he raised the objection regarding death and knowledge. His statement: (with the fact that they are not opposites according to you due to the absence of impossibility of their coexistence in themselves, but it is not hidden that there is then no benefit in restricting it with his words 'according to you'. His statement: (and there is not) the preventing factor from that is their opposition, because the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to themselves, but rather due to the impossibility of the coexistence of their two rulings between them. His statement: (The objection is raised against them regarding death and life) if it is established that death is existent, and that they do not claim opposition. His statement: (The author of al-Quniyyah said etc.) It was said: The commentator considered unlikely the non-consideration of death as an opposite to life on the assumption of its existence, so he transmitted the words of al-Quniyyah as an indication of a possible defect in the transmission from the author, for his statement is about knowledge and death, not about death and life, but it is refuted from him by considering the condition of 'in themselves' in the definition of opposites; since the non-coexistence of death and knowledge is not due to themselves, and the author changed his statement for that reason. The truth is that what the author mentioned is taken from Abkar al-Afkar, for the objection there is about death and life.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The commentator first explains the objection: the opponent argues that if death and life are not considered opposites (ḍiddān) by the Ash'aris, yet they cannot coexist, then why must knowledge and ignorance be considered opposites? The impossibility of coexistence does not necessitate opposition (taḍādd). The commentator clarifies that this objection is a 'prevention' (manʿ) and a 'support' (sanad) for their own position, not a refutation (naqḍ). Then the commentator quotes Ṣāḥib al-Qunyah, who raised a similar objection comparing knowledge and death. The response is that the impossibility of coexistence of knowledge and ignorance is not due to their essences (dhātayhimā) but due to the impossibility of combining their rulings (ḥukmayhimā). The commentator also discusses whether the objection truly applies, noting that the author's argument is ultimately derived from Abkār al-Afkār, where the same point about death and life is made.

Key Terms
ضدان (ḍiddān)
تضاد (taḍādd)
امتناع اجتماع (imtināʿ al-ijtimāʿ)
ذات (dhāt)
حكم (ḥukm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things cannot coexist, they must be opposites.
Correction: Impossibility of coexistence can have different grounds; opposition is specifically when the impossibility arises from the essences themselves, not from external factors like rulings.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection using death and life?
A) Death and life are opposites according to the Ash'aris.
B) The impossibility of coexistence does not necessarily require opposition.
C) Knowledge and ignorance are not opposites because death and life are not opposites.
D) The Ash'aris deny the existence of death.

Key Takeaway
The opposition of knowledge and ignorance is grounded in the impossibility of combining their rulings, not merely their essences, thus preserving the doctrine against the death-life analogy.

Describing Divine Attributes as Similar or Different
Al-Jurjani discusses two views on describing God's attributes as similar/different.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars deny describing God's attributes as similar or different, while others affirm it, and how the concept of otherness (al-ghayrayn) applies.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not separable, so similarity and difference, which require otherness, may not apply.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a nuanced theological debate: can we say that God's eternal attributes are similar to or different from each other? The answer hinges on how we understand 'otherness' (taghayyur). Some scholars argue that since attributes are not separate entities, these categories don't apply. Others, like al-Qadi, think we can still speak of difference based on each attribute's unique essence. Let's follow the argument as it unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Introduce the problem
Before reading, consider that divine attributes are eternal and inseparable from God's essence. Can we compare them to each other? If two things are different, they must be distinct—but are the attributes distinct?
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the two opposing positions presented. Notice the key terms: al-ghayrayn (the two others), taghayyur (otherness), tamāthul (similarity), ikhtilāf (difference).
The passage cites al-Amidi's report from al-Qadi.
3
Identify the two positions
Distinguish the first group (who deny similarity/difference because attributes lack otherness) from the second (who affirm it because attributes are other/mutaghayira). Then note al-Qadi's special view: difference based on essential attributes, not requiring 'otherness' in the sense of separability.
4
Analyze the clarification
The passage ends with a technical note: the concept of al-ghayrayn (two others) is essential. If every pair of attributes are considered 'two others,' then they would be described as different; but if the terms are restricted to separable things, then attributes (inseparable from the essence) would not be described as either similar or different.
This clarifies the nuance in applying these categories.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate affects our understanding of divine simplicity. Does affirming difference among attributes compromise God's oneness? How do different schools of kalam resolve this?
This connects to broader theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الاختلاف في الغيرين عائد هاهنا، فمنهم من لا يصف الصفات ) أي صفات الله تعالى القديمة ( بالتماثل والاختلاف، بناء على أنهما من أقسام التغاير، ولا تغاير بين الصفات كما مر . ( ومنهم من يصفها بهما ) ، بناء على أن تلك الصفات متغايرة، هذا المتبادر من عبارة الكتاب ونقل الآمدي عن القاضي القول بالاختلاف، نظراً إلى ما اختص به كل صفة تلك الصفات، من من صفة نفسية من غير التفات إلى وصف الغيرية وعلى هذا، فالقاضي لا يشترط الغيرية في التخالف فبالأولى أن لا يشترطها. قوله : ( واعلم أن الاختلاف في الغيرين إلخ) أي مفهوم الغيرين عائد هاهنا أي في التماثل والاختلاف، فإنه لا بد في الاتصاف بهما من الاثنينية، وإن كان كل اثنين غيرين تكون صفاته تعالى متصفة بأحدهما، وإن خصا بما يجوز الانفكاك بينهما لا تكون متصفة بشيء منهما، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم .
And know that the disagreement concerning 'the two others' (al-ghayrayn) returns here. So some of them do not describe the attributes (i.e., God's eternal attributes) with similarity and difference, based on the grounds that they are divisions of otherness, and there is no otherness between the attributes as has passed. And some of them describe them with both, based on the grounds that those attributes are other (mutaghayira). This is the apparent meaning from the wording of the book, and al-Amidi reported from al-Qadi the opinion of difference, considering what each attribute is distinguished by of an essential attribute, without regard to the description of otherness. And on this, al-Qadi does not require otherness in difference (takhāluf), so a fortiori he does not require it. His statement: 'And know that the disagreement concerning al-ghayrayn, etc.': i.e., the concept of al-ghayrayn returns here, i.e., in similarity and difference, for it is necessary in being described with them to have duality, and if every two are 'two others,' then His attributes (Exalted) would be described with one of them; but if they are specified with that which allows separation between them, they would not be described with any of them. This is how it should be understood.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether God's eternal attributes can be described as 'similar' or 'different' to each other. It presents two positions: some deny these descriptions because they are divisions of 'otherness' (taghayyur), and there is no otherness between the attributes (since they are not separate entities). Others affirm these descriptions because they consider the attributes to be distinct from each other. The author notes that this is the apparent meaning of the text, and al-Amidi reports that al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) held the opinion of 'difference' (ikhtilāf) due to each attribute having a unique essential attribute, without requiring 'otherness' in the sense of being separate. Thus, al-Qadi does not require otherness for difference, and by implication also for similarity. The commentary clarifies that the concept of 'the two others' is relevant here: to be described as similar or different, there must be a duality (two things). If every pair of attributes are considered 'two others,' then they would be described with one of them (i.e., they would be different). If these terms are restricted to things that can be separated, then attributes (which are inseparable from the divine essence) would not be described as either. This highlights the nuance in applying these categories to the divine attributes.

Key Terms
الغيرين
التغاير
التخالف
صفات الله
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two attributes are different, they must be separable or distinct existences.
Correction: Al-Qadi shows that difference can be based on essential characteristics (nafs al-sifa) without requiring otherness in the sense of being separate entities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some scholars not describe God's attributes as similar or different?
A) Because they believe attributes are not real.
B) Because similarity and difference require otherness, which does not apply to the attributes.
C) Because they consider all attributes identical.
D) Because they follow al-Qadi.

Key Takeaway
Whether divine attributes can be described as similar or different depends on whether 'otherness' is required; al-Qadi argues for difference based on essential distinctness without requiring separability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Qadi argued for difference without requiring otherness—a subtle distinction. When you encounter theological complexities that seem abstract, do you tend to dismiss them as irrelevant, or do you grapple with them? Al-Ghayrayn and Similarity of Attributes
Al-Isfahani explains al-ghayrayn in similarity and difference, and the ruling on 'servant similar to Lord'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of al-ghayrayn (the two others) governs similarity and difference among divine attributes, and why it is permissible to say the created is similar to the Lord but not vice versa.

Core Idea
Similarity requires otherness; divine attributes lack otherness, so they are not similar or different.

This passage from al-Isfahani's commentary tackles a subtle but important point in Islamic theology: whether the eternal attributes of God can be described as similar or different from each other. The answer hinges on the concept of al-ghayrayn (the two others), which requires a certain kind of separability. The passage also addresses the asymmetric permissibility of saying the created is similar to the Lord, based on revelatory language.

Guided Path
1
Understand the debate on attributes
Read the first part of the passage where scholars disagree about describing attributes with similarity/difference. Notice that the disagreement stems from whether otherness (al-taghāyur) exists between the attributes.
2
Identify al-Qāḍī's view
Al-Āmidī reports that al-Qāḍī does not require the description of otherness for difference; he looks at what each attribute is in itself. Understand that this leads to a different conclusion.
Al-Qāḍī says the attributes differ based on their own specific properties, not due to otherness.
3
Grasp the condition of twoness
The commentary explains that similarity and difference require twoness. If two things are inseparable (like attributes), they cannot be described as 'two others' and therefore cannot be similar or different.
This is a technical point: al-ghayrayn implies separability.
4
Apply the asymmetric ruling
The passage ends with a ruling: it is permissible to say the created is similar to the Lord, but not the reverse. Understand the reasoning: the application of the word is based on revelation and does not imply the converse.
This respects divine transcendence while allowing limited similarity in expression.

Know that the disagreement concerning the two others (al-ghayrayn) returns here. Among them are those who do not describe the attributes (i.e., the eternal attributes of God, Most High) with similarity and difference, on the basis that they are divisions of otherness (al-taghāyur), and there is no otherness between the attributes as previously mentioned. And among them are those who describe them with both, on the basis that those attributes are mutually other (mutaghāyira). This is the apparent meaning of the book's expression, and al-Āmidī reported from al-Qāḍī the statement of difference, considering what each attribute of those attributes is distinguished by, from an attribute of the self, without paying attention to the description of otherness. On this, al-Qāḍī does not require otherness in opposition (al-takhāluf), so a fortiori he does not require it ... Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of al-ghayrayn (the two others) in the concepts of similarity and difference. The author explains that the disagreement about whether divine attributes can be described as similar or different stems from the notion of otherness (al-taghāyur). Some scholars do not describe the attributes with similarity or difference because they consider these as categories of otherness, and there is no otherness among the attributes (as previously established). Others describe them with both, holding that the attributes are mutually other. The author notes that al-Āmidī reported from al-Qāḍī the view that the attributes differ based on what each is distinguished by, without requiring the description of otherness. This implies that al-Qāḍī does not require otherness for opposition, and a fortiori not for similarity. Then the commentary (qāla) clarifies that 'similar' here is restricted to the specific attribute, not absolute, and that the concept of al-ghayrayn requires twoness. If two things are separable, they can be described as two others; if inseparable (like attributes), they are not described as such. Finally, an important ruling is given: if one prevents applying the word 'similar' to the Lord, it may still be permissible to say the created is similar to the Lord, because that application does not imply the converse. This is based on the principle of tawqīf (revelation-based naming).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If it is impermissible to say 'the Lord is similar to the created', then it must also be impermissible to say 'the created is similar to the Lord'.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the prohibition is asymmetric: we may say the created is similar to the Lord because that application does not necessitate the reverse. The ruling is based on revelation and reverence, not logical symmetry.

Checkpoint

C) Because the attributes are all the same in meaning.
D) Because the Quran forbids such descriptions.

Key Takeaway
The concepts of similarity and difference require otherness and separability, which do not apply to God's attributes, and the permissibility of describing creatures as similar to God is based on revelation, not logical symmetry.

Ash'ari Real Attributes and Divine Causality
Commentator clarifies that Ash'ari belief in real attributes allows multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari affirmation of real attributes does not violate the principle of divine simplicity but rather explains how multiple effects can proceed from God.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari real attributes allow multiple effects without making God composed.

The commentator warns against a potential confusion: if God has real attributes, does that make Him composed? The Ash'aris affirm attributes to explain how many diverse effects can come from one God, while preserving that He is not a composite in the sense of having parts. This passage clarifies that even one attribute suffices to allow two effects: one from essence, another from attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator uses a warning phrase 'ولا يلتبس عليك' to introduce the point.
2
Understand the philosophical background
Reflect on the phrase 'لم يكن هو بسيطاً' (He is not simple). In philosophy, a simple being has no multiplicity of aspects. The commentator is addressing a worry that attributes would compromise simplicity.
3
Analyze the explanation of multiple effects
Follow the argument: multiplicity of attributes is not necessary; one attribute suffices for two effects. One effect from the essence, another from the attribute. This shows how the Ash'ari doctrine is consistent.
Consider how this answers the philosophers' objection that a simple being can only produce one effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلتبس عليك أن الأشاعرة لما أثبتوا له تعالى صفات حقيقة، لم يكن هو بسيطاً، لأن تعدد أحد هذه الأمور غير لازم بل واحد منها يكفي في صدور أثرين، بأن يكون صدور واحد منهما من حيث ذاته وصدور آخر من حيث أحد هذه الأمور.

Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a clarifying note from the commentator addressing a potential confusion. The commentator warns the reader not to misunderstand how the Ash'ari position fits with the philosophers' rule about the simple being. The Ash'aris affirm real attributes (صفات حقيقة) for God, such as knowledge, power, will, etc. Because of these attributes, God is not 'simple' in the philosophers' strict sense—that is, without any multiplicity at all. The commentator explains that the Ash'ari view does not require that every aspect be multiple; rather, even a single real attribute is sufficient for two effects to proceed: one from the essence directly, and another from the attribute. Thus the Ash'ari doctrine that all possible things are directly caused by God is consistent, because the multiplicity of attributes provides the necessary aspectual multiplicity to allow for multiple effects.

Misconception: Some might think that affirming real attributes makes God composed of parts or compromises His unity.
Correction: The Ash'aris hold that real attributes are not parts but aspects that are distinct from the essence yet not separate. This does not make God composed; it allows for multiple effects without dividing the essence.

The False Impression about Necessary vs. Free Cause
Refutes the false impression that a necessary cause can only produce one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rule 'the one does not produce more than one' does not apply to God and how the distinction between necessitating and free cause is a false dichotomy in this context.

Core Idea
The rule only applies to what is one from all aspects; God's attributes make Him not subject to that rule.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a common confusion about causality and divine attributes. Some thinkers wrongly imagine that if God were a necessitating cause, He could only produce one effect, and only as a free agent could He produce many. The commentator shows why this reasoning is flawed and clarifies the true scope of the rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument against the false impression.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the false impression
Find where the text says 'وقد يتوهم' (it may be falsely imagined). This is the core misunderstanding being addressed.
The false impression is that a necessitating cause can only produce one effect.
3
Understand the commentator's refutation
The commentator argues that a free agent with multiple wills is not one from all aspects, so the rule does not apply. Also, God's attributes are not separate from His essence.
This shows why the rule cannot be used to argue about God's causality.
4
Connect to Ash'ari doctrine
Note the reference to attributes being necessities of the essence. This is key to understanding why the false impression is invalid.

truly one from all aspects, so it does not fall under their view in this rule. Some falsely imagine that if the cause is necessitating (موجب), only one effect can proceed, but if it is free (مختار), multiple effects are possible. The commentator then reminds the reader that according to the Ash'aris, God's attributes are not other than His essence, so they do not proceed from Him as effects; rather they are necessities of the essence. The First Proof: A Single Real Agent Cannot Be the Source of Two Distinct Effects
Al-Iji presents the first proof that a single real agent cannot produce two effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a single, simple being cannot be the source of two distinct effects, as it leads to either composition or infinite regress.

Core Idea
A single simple being cannot produce two effects; it leads to contradiction.

This lesson examines the first proof presented by al-Iji in his theological work. The argument demonstrates that if a single real agent were the source of two distinct effects, then its sourcehood for each effect would be distinct concepts. The key question is whether these concepts are internal or external to the agent, leading to either composition or infinite regress. By the end, you will see why a simple being must have only one effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the initial conditional statement: if a single real being were the source of two effects, then the sourcehoods are distinct.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum structure.
2
Identify the two branches
The proof splits into two possibilities: the concepts of sourcehood either enter into the real being or they do not. If they do, composition results; if not, regress follows.
Understanding this branching is crucial.
3
Examine the six cases
The text simplifies into six scenarios based on whether each sourcehood concept enters, exits, or is identical to the real being. Work through each case to see how it leads to composition, regress, or both.
All six are shown to be impossible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since all possibilities lead to contradiction, the original assumption that a single real being can have two distinct effects is false. Reflect on why this matters for divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول لو كان الواحد الحقيقي مصدراً لـ (ا) و لـ(ب) مثلاً لكان مصدرية (ا) غير مصدرية (ب)؛ لإمكان تعقل كل منهما بدون الأخرى، فإن دخل فيه أي في الواحد الحقيقي (هما) أي هذان المفهومان ( أو ) دخل فيه أحدهما لزم التركيب في الواحد الحقيقي، هذا خلف . ( وإلا ) وإن لم يدخل فيه هذان ولا أحدهما، (لكان ذلك الواحد الحقيقي مصدراً لمصدريتهما) أي لمصدريتي (ا) و (ب)، كما كان مصدراً لهما؛ إذ لا يجوز أن تكون المصدريتان مستندتين إلى غيره وإلا لم يكن هو وحده مصدراً لـ(ا) و (ب)، والمقدر خلافه . (و) حينئذ (عاد الكلام فيهما أي في المصدريتين، فنقول : كونه مصدراً لإحدى المصدريتين، غير كونه مصدراً للأخرى، فهذان المفهومان إن دخلا فيه أو أحدهما لزم التركيب، وإلا كان مصدراً لهما أيضاً، ولزم التسلسل في المصدريات، وقد يُقرر هذا الوجه بطريق أبسط، فيقال : إن كان كل من مفهومي مصدرية (ا) ، ومصدرية (ب) نفس الواحد الحقيقي كان لأمر بسيط ماهيتان مختلفتان وإن دخلا فيه معا، أو دخل أحدهما وكان الآخر عيناً لزم التركيب فقط . وإن خرجا معاً، أو خرج أحدهما وكان الآخر عيناً لزم التسلسل فقط، وإن دخل أحدهما وخرج الآخر لزم التركيب والتسلسل معاً، فالأقسام ستة، والكل محال
The first proof: If the single real being were a source for (a) and (b) for example, then its being a source for (a) would be different from its being a source for (b), because each can be conceptualized without the other. If these two concepts enter into it (i.e., into the single real being) or one of them enters into it, then composition follows in the single real being, which is a contradiction. If neither enters into it, then that single real being would be a source for the two sourcehoods, as it was a source for the two effects, because it is not permissible for the two sourcehoods to be attributed to something else, otherwise it alone would not be the source for (a) and (b), contrary to what is supposed. Then the argument returns to the two sourcehoods: its being a source for one sourcehood is different from its being a source for the other. If these two concepts enter into it or one enters, composition follows; otherwise it would be a source for them both, and infinite regress follows in sourcehoods. This argument can be presented more simply: if each of the two concepts of sourcehood for (a) and sourcehood for (b) is identical to the single real being, then a simple thing would have two different essences. If both enter into it, or one enters while the other is identical, only composition follows. If both exit, or one exits while the other is identical, only regress follows. If one enters and the other exits, both composition and regress follow. Thus there are six cases, all impossible.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the possibility of a single real agent producing multiple effects. The argument is structured as a conditional: if a single real being were the source of two distinct effects (a and b), then the sourcehood for each must be distinct, since each can be conceived independently. The key question is whether these two concepts (sourcehood for a and sourcehood for b) are within the real being or separate. If they are within, then the real being would be composed of these concepts, contradicting its simplicity. If they are separate, then the real being is the source for the sourcehoods themselves, leading to an infinite regress of sourcehoods. The argument then enumerates six possible scenarios based on whether each concept enters, exits, or is identical to the real being. In each case, either composition or regress or both follow, all of which are impossible. Therefore, the initial assumption that a single real being can be the source of two distinct effects is false. The passage uses precise logical reasoning to demonstrate the impossibility, relying on the principles of non-composition and non-regress.

Key Terms
الواحد الحقيقي
مصدرية
التركيب
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simple being can have multiple relational attributes without composition.
Correction: The argument shows that if these relations are distinct concepts, they either enter into the being (causing composition) or are separate (leading to regress). Neither is acceptable for a simple being.

Checkpoint
How many possible cases does the first proof enumerate regarding the relationship between the two sourcehood concepts and the single real being?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
A single simple being cannot be the source of two distinct effects because any such scenario leads to either composition or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is absolutely simple and the sole source of all things, how does that affect your tendency to attribute outcomes to secondary causes (like your own effort or other people) rather than seeing Him as the ultimate source? Will you consciously trace every effect back to Him alone starting today?
?
Consider an area of your life where you see two different 'causes' operating—for example, your success in work and your health. Do you ever treat these as coming from separate sources within your mind? How does the impossibility of composition in the divine essence challenge you to unify your reliance on Allah alone?
?
This proof shows that multiplicity leads to infinite regress or contradiction. Is there any belief or practice in your life where you quietly assume multiple 'gods'—like fearing people as much as Allah, or trusting in wealth alongside Allah? What concrete step can you take today to eliminate that hidden shirk?

Negative Attributes and Will Enable Multiplicity
Al-Amidi argues that negative attributes and will allow one essence to produce many effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine essence, despite being one, can produce multiple effects through its many negative attributes and a will with multiple attachments.

Core Idea
The one God creates many things through negative attributes and will.

This lesson presents Al-Amidi's defense of the possibility of multiple effects from a single divine essence. The author argues that the divine essence is described with many negative attributes, such as not being a substance or accident, which serve as distinct aspects for causation. Additionally, God has a will that attaches to multiple things, allowing multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and locate the rhetorical question: why can't one essence have multiple specific relations? This is the core claim being defended.
The passage begins with 'الحقيقية. قلنا : لم لا يجوز'.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that each effect requires a unique specific relation, which would imply composition in the essence. Find where the author grants this possibility ('ولئن سلم').
3
Analyze the answer
The answer uses two concepts: many negative attributes (سلوب كثيرة) and a will with multiple attachments (إرادة يتعدد تعلقها). These allow the essence to relate to multiple effects without composition.
Negative attributes are real aspects like 'not a body', not mere privations.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why this argument is important: it preserves divine simplicity while allowing God to create a diverse world without intermediaries.
This connects to broader debates against Aristotelian and Neoplatonic causality.

إعادة لما ذكره بقوله : ولا يلتبس عليك إلخ. ولو قال : فذلك لا ينفعكم، لأن المبدأ الحقيقي متصف في نفس الأمر بسلوب كثيرة، فيكون هذا الحكم لغواً من الكلام، لكان جواباً آخر. قوله: بسلوب كثيرة لم يتعرض للإضافات) ؛ لأنه يمكن المناقشة فيها بأن فرع تحقق الطرفين، ولم يتحقق معه شيء، لا ذهناً ولا خارجاً. وما قيل : من أنه إذا اعتبر ذاته تعالى في مرتبة لم يمكن حينئذ سلب ولا وجود والكلام في أنه تعالى في هذه المرتبة لا يصدر عنه أمران توهم محض؛ لأن هذا الاعتبار فرض للشيء بدون ما يقتضيه ذاته تعالى. وحينئذ لا يصدر عنه شيء؛ لامتناع وجوده بهذا الاعتبار، فإن ذاته تعالى يلزمه في نفس الأمر سلوب مثل أن وجوده وتعينه ليس زائداً عليه، وأنه ليس بجوهر ولا عرض وإن كان الحكم بلزومه موقوفاً على التعقل، فاعتبار تجرده عنها فرض محال مستلزماً للمحال، هو امتناع صدور أثر عنه، فتدبر فإنه مما خفي على أقوام . الإشارات ورد عليه : بأنه إذا حمل هذا الحكم على ما يفهم من الألفاظ المعبر بها عنه، فلا نزاع في قربه من الوضوح لأنه إذا اعتبر الوحدة المجردة التي لا يكون فيها ولا معها تعدد بوجه من الوجوه، ولو بتعدد القوابل لم يتصور صدور المتعدد، وكيف يتصور صدور غير القابل من الفاعل . لكن يكون هذا حكماً لغواً لا فائدة فيه أصلاً؛ إذ لا يصدق الواحد بهذا المعنى على شيء من الأشياء، لا في الخارج ولا في العقل إلا بطريق القرض وإنما كثر مدافعة الناس في أن الواحد الحقيقي الذي هو الله تعالى على ما هو عليه في نفس الأمر من أحواله بعد التنزل، وتسليم كونه موجباً بالذات، وأن ليس له صفات موجودة، هل يجوز أن يصدر عنه متعدد أم لا؟ فنحن نقول : نعم ، كيف لا وله ذات وجود مطلق زائد على ذاته عند الفلاسفة أيضاً؟ . قوله : (قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يكون لذات واحدة إلخ) ولو سلم فلم لا يجوز أن يكون للفاعل البسيط مع أحد معلوليه خصوصية صدور هذا عنه خصوصية مع الآخر، وهكذا. فيكون كل الممكنات مستندة إلى الله تعالى بهذا الطريق، لا كما قالت الفلاسفة واشتهر عنهم من حوادث عالم العناصر إلى العقل الفعال واستناد بعض العقول والأفلاك إلى عقل آخر، كما سيأتي تفصيله The real. We say: why is it not permissible that one essence has a specific relation with multiple things sharing in one aspect or not sharing in it, such that that specific relation is not present with other than those things, and then those things all proceed from it, but some without others. And even if it is granted that a specific relation with each proceeding thing is necessary, that does not harm us, because the true source is in reality described with many negative attributes; rather it has a will with multiple attachments, so it is permissible that things proceed from it from these aspects. His saying: (And if it is granted etc.) is a repetition of what he mentioned by his saying: Do not be confused etc. And if he said: That does not benefit you, because the true source is in reality described with many negative attributes, so this judgment would be meaningless speech, that would be another answer. His saying: by many negative attributes he did not address the relations; because it can be discussed in them that they branch from the realization of the two sides, and nothing is realized with it, neither mentally nor externally. And what is said: that if His essence, may He be exalted, is considered in a rank, then neither negation nor existence is possible, and the discussion is that He, may He be exalted, in this rank does not produce two things, is pure illusion; because this consideration is a supposition of the thing without what His essence requires. Then nothing proceeds from it; due to the impossibility of its existence in this consideration. For His essence, may He be exalted, necessitates in reality negative attributes, such that His existence and individuation are not additional to Him, and that He is not a substance nor an accident, even if the judgment of its necessity is dependent on intellection. So considering it devoid of them is an impossible supposition that entails an impossible thing, which is the impossibility of an effect proceeding from it. So reflect, for it is something hidden from peoples. The indications and objection to it: that if this judgment is taken in the sense understood from the words used to express it, there is no dispute about its nearness to clarity, because if one considers the absolute unity which has no multiplicity in itself or with it in any way, even by the multiplicity of recipients, the multiplicity of effects is inconceivable. But this is a futile judgment with no benefit at all; for the one in this sense is not true of anything, neither in the external nor in the intellect except by way of assumption. Rather people's disputation is about the true one, who is God Almighty, as He is in reality in His states after descending, and granting that He is necessitating by essence, and that He has no existing attributes: is it permissible that a multiplicity should proceed from Him or not? So we say: yes, why not, since He has an absolute existence additional to His essence also according to the philosophers. His saying: (We said why is it not permissible that one essence etc.) And even if granted, why is it not permissible that the simple agent has with one of its effects a specific relation of this effect proceeding from it, a specific relation with the other, and so on. Thus all contingent things are dependent on God Almighty by this way, not as the philosophers said and as is famous among them of the events of the elemental world coming from the active intellect and the dependence of some intellects and spheres on another intellect, as will be detailed. His saying: (Because the true source is in reality described with many negative attributes) contains a refutation of what is said: intellection of negation is dependent on the affirmation of the other; so if negation had a role in its affirmation, it would be circular. And the refutation is that...

Commentary
This passage provides a multifaceted defense of the possibility of multiple effects from a single divine essence. The author argues that the divine essence has many negative attributes (سلوب), such as not being a substance or accident, which serve as distinct aspects allowing for multiple effects. Additionally, God has a will with multiple attachments, which further allows for multiplicity. The author refutes the objection that considering God in a state of absolute unity would preclude any effect, by stating that such a consideration is impossible because the divine essence necessarily implies negative attributes. The passage concludes by affirming that all contingent things depend directly on God, contrary to the philosophical view of a hierarchy of intermediaries.

Misconception: Negative attributes are mere privations with no real causal role.
Correction: The author treats negative attributes as real aspects (لوازم ذاتية) of the divine essence that allow distinctions in causation.

Checkpoint

The divine essence's negative attributes and will enable it to cause many different things without compromising its simplicity.

Negative Attributes and the Impossibility of Absolute Unity
Al-Amidi defends negative attributes and rejects the concept of absolute unity as impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative attributes are not dependent on external existence and why considering God in absolute unity is an impossible supposition.

Core Idea
Negative attributes are real aspects of God, not dependent on external relations.

This lesson explores the nature of negative attributes and answers the objection that they presuppose external things. The author also argues that considering God in a state of absolute unity (without any negation) is impossible, because the divine essence necessarily implies negative attributes such as 'not a substance' or 'not an accident'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the passage starts with a discussion about whether negative attributes require external relations (إضافات).
The commentator notes that the author did not mention relations, which can be problematic.
2
Understand the objection about negation dependency
Some argue that intellection of negation (salb) depends on the affirmation of the other (thubut al-ghayr), creating a potential circularity. Identify this objection in the text.
The phrase 'تعقل السلب موقوف على ثبوت الغير' captures this.
3
Grasp the argument against absolute unity
The author refutes the notion that God can be considered in a state of pure unity (الوحدة المجردة) without any negatives. This is impossible because His essence necessitates negatives; without them, no effect can proceed.
The text says considering God devoid of negatives is 'فرض محال' (an impossible supposition).
4
Note the philosophers' concession
The passage concludes with a partial agreement: the philosophers also admit that God has an absolute existence additional to His essence, which supports the author's position.
This sets up the next lesson on direct causation.

?

?

?

Direct Divine Causality Refutes Philosophical Hierarchy
Al-Amidi asserts direct divine causation and begins refuting the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the simple agent can be directly responsible for all contingent things without intermediaries, and how the use of negative attributes avoids circularity.

Core Idea
God directly causes all things through His essence and negative attributes.

This lesson concludes Al-Amidi's defense by asserting that all contingent things depend directly on God through specific relations, not through a chain of intermediaries as the philosophers claimed. It also addresses a circularity objection regarding negative attributes, showing that the refutation relies on the intrinsic nature of the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the opening where the author argues that a simple agent can have specific relations with each effect. Find the phrase 'الفاعل البسيط' (the simple agent).
This is a direct response to the philosophers' hierarchy of intellects.
2
Contrast with philosophical view
Note the author's rejection of the chain from active intellect to elemental world. He insists all contingent things are directly from God.
The text says 'لا كما قالت الفلاسفة'.
3
Understand the circularity objection
The passage ends with a new objection: if intellection of negation depends on affirmation of another, and negation is used to affirm causality, it would be circular. Identify this in the text.
The phrase 'تعقل السلب موقوف على ثبوت الغير' reappears.
4
Note the refutation begins
The passage begins the refutation by stating that the true source has many negative attributes in reality, which breaks the alleged circularity. However, the text cuts off; the student is to understand that the refutation is based on the self-sufficiency of negative attributes.
The end of the passage is incomplete in the source, but the direction is clear.

Commentary
This passage provides a multifaceted defense of the possibility of multiple effects from a single divine essence. The author argues that the divine essence has many negative attributes (سلوب), such as not being a substance or accident, which serve as distinct aspects allowing for multiple effects. Additionally, God has a will with multiple attachments, which further allows for multiplicity. The author refutes the objection that considering God in a state of absolute unity would preclude any effect, by stating that such a consideration is impossible because the divine essence necessarily implies negative attributes. The passage concludes by affirming that all contingent things depend directly on God, contrary to the philosophical view of a hierarchy of intermediaries.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God needs intermediaries to create many different things.
Correction: The author argues that God, as a simple agent, can directly create all things through specific relations without intermediaries.

Checkpoint

A) Through a chain of intellects and spheres.
B) Directly through specific relations from the simple agent.
C) Through the active intellect only.
D) Through the will of angels.

Key Takeaway
God directly causes all contingent things through His essence and negative attributes, without intermediaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change in your daily attitude if you truly internalized that there is no intermediary between you and His decree?
?
This lesson argues that God's negative attributes (like 'not a body') are not merely derived from creation but are intrinsic to His essence. When you think about God's transcendence, do you reduce Him to what He is not, or do you let that negation drive you to affirm His unique greatness in worship? How can you actively cultivate awe of His incomparability today?

Multiple Effects from One Cause via Particularity and Negations
Jurjani presents the first part of his response to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single simple divine essence can produce multiple effects through its particularity with different things and its negative attributes.

Core Idea
Multiple effects from one cause are possible through different aspects and negations.

Jurjani begins by directly addressing the objection that a single simple essence cannot produce multiple effects. He argues that the essence can have a particularity with multiple things, allowing them all to proceed from it through different aspects. This lesson explores that initial response, focusing on the role of particularity and negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation. Pay attention to how Jurjani structures his response.
The passage begins with the objection implied in the question.
2
Identify the two parts of the response
Find where Jurjani first gives a conditional response ('why not?') and then concedes a point but answers with negations and will.
3
Understand the concept of 'particularity'
Reflect on how a single essence can have a special relation with each effect, allowing multiplicity without division.
The essence remains one; the particularities are aspects of relation.
4
Note the introduction of negations and will
See how Jurjani uses the fact that the essence is described with many negations (سلوب) and has a will with multiple attachments to allow different effects to proceed.
Negations are real attributes that do not compromise divine simplicity.

So we say: Yes, why not, seeing that He has an absolute existence additional to His essence according to the philosophers as well? Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the claim that a single simple cause can produce multiple effects. The author begins by stating the objection: how can a single essence have a particularity with multiple things? He then gives multiple responses. First, the essence can have special relations with different things, so that from different aspects it produces different effects. Even if each effect requires a specific relation, the essence is described in reality with many negative attributes (سلوب)—such as that its existence is not additional to it, that it is not a substance or accident—and it has a will with multiple attachments, allowing production from these aspects. He then addresses the claim that considering the essence in a rank of absolute unity would prevent multiplicity, arguing that such a consideration is a mere supposition and not real; the essence necessarily entails negations in reality. He also refutes the charge of circularity: the conceptualization of a negation depends on the conceptualization of the negated thing, not on its external existence, so negation does not need a pre-existing opposite. The passage concludes that all these negations and relations do not detract from God being truly one in essence.

سلوب
إضافات
صدور
ذات
إرادة
خصوصية

Checkpoint
What does Jurjani say about the true principle (God) that allows it to produce multiple effects even if a particularity is required for each?
A) It is composed of parts.
B) It has many negations and a will with multiple attachments.
C) It is not truly one.
D) It produces effects in different times.

Key Takeaway
A single simple essence can produce multiple effects through its particularity with each effect and through its negative attributes and multiple will-attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often reduce God to simple formulas. This lesson shows that even negations—what God is not—are sources of existence. What specific negations (e.g., 'God is not unjust') have you ignored that actually reveal His majesty and power? How must your concept of God expand today?
?
The will of God has countless attachments. When you make du'a, do you realize your request is just one of an infinite number of attachments? Does that humble you or make you feel insignificant? How can this truth drive you to be more specific and earnest in your supplication?
?
If you truly believe that God's simplicity does not limit His effects, why do you sometimes feel that your situation is hopeless? What 'particularities' has God placed in your life—unique circumstances, trials, or blessings—that you have overlooked as channels of His action? Take time today to identify one and use it as a lens to see His activity.

Refuting the Objection of Absolute Unity and Circularity
Jurjani refutes two specific objections: the absolute unity argument and the circularity of negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that absolute unity prevents multiplicity is a mere supposition and how negations are not conceptually circular.

Core Idea
Absolute unity is a useful fiction; real divine unity is compatible with negations.

In this continuation, Jurjani addresses two further objections: first, that if we consider the essence in absolute unity, no multiplicity can proceed; second, that negations require the existence of their opposites, leading to circularity. He refutes both, showing that the former is a mere supposition and the latter rests on a confusion between conceptualization and external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
This passage is dense; read slowly. Notice the sections addressing different objections.
The passage includes a quote from the opponent and Jurjani's reply.
2
Understand the first objection about absolute unity
Identify why the opponent argues that considering the essence in absolute unity would prevent any production.
The objection claims that if the essence is considered stripped of all attributes, it cannot produce.
3
See Jurjani's refutation of that objection
He argues that such a consideration is a mere supposition (فرض) and not real; the essence necessarily entails negations in reality.
The essence cannot exist without those negations, so the consideration is impossible.
4
Examine the circularity objection and its refutation
The opponent argues that understanding a negation requires knowing the negated thing, so negation cannot be prior. Jurjani responds that only conceptualization is needed, not external existence.
The phrase 'وجه الدفع أن أمور كثيرة' is incomplete but indicates multiple points of refutation.

لغواً من الكلام، لكان جواباً آخر. قوله: بسلوب كثيرة لم يتعرض للإضافات) ؛ لأنه يمكن المناقشة فيها بأن فرع تحقق الطرفين، ولم يتحقق معه شيء، لا ذهناً ولا خارجاً. وما قيل : من أنه إذا اعتبر ذاته تعالى في الاعتبار، فإن ذاته تعالى يلزمه في نفس الأمر سلوب مثل أن وجوده وتعينه ليس زائداً عليه، وأنه ليس بجوهر ولا عرض وإن كان الحكم بلزومه موقوفاً على التعقل، فاعتبار تجرده عنها فرض محال مستلزماً للمحال، هو امتناع صدور أثر عنه، فتدبر فإنه مما خفي على الأقوام. الإشارات ورد عليه : بأنه إذا حمل هذا الحكم على ما يفهم من الألفاظ المعبر بها عنه، فلا نزاع في قربه من الوضوح لأنه إذا اعتبر الوحدة المجردة التي لا يكون فيها ولا معها تعدد بوجه من الوجوه، ولو بتعدد القوابل لم يتصور صدور المتعدد، وكيف يتصور صدور غير القابل من الفاعل . لكن يكون هذا حكماً لغواً لا فائدة فيه أصلاً؛ إذ لا يصدق الواحد بهذا المعنى على شيء من الأشياء، لا في الخارج ولا في العقل إلا بطريق القرض وإنما كثر مدافعة الناس في أن الواحد الحقيقي الذي هو الله تعالى على ما هو عليه في نفس الأمر من أحواله بعد التنزل، وتسليم كونه موجباً بالذات، وأن ليس له صفات موجودة، هل يجوز أن يصدر عنه متعدد أم لا؟ فنحن نقول : نعم ، كيف لا وله ذات وجود مطلق زائد على ذاته عند الفلاسفة أيضاً؟ . قوله : (قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يكون لذات واحدة إلخ) ولو سلم فلم لا يجوز أن يكون للفاعل البسيط مع أحد معلوليه خصوصية صدور هذا عنه خصوصية مع الآخر، وهكذا. فيكون كل الممكنات مستندة إلى الله تعالى بهذا الطريق، لا كما قالت الفلاسفة واشتهر عنهم من حوادث عالم العناصر إلى العقل الفعال واستناد بعض العقول والأفلاك إلى عقل آخر، كما سيأتي تفصيله. قوله : ( لأن المبدأ الحقيقي متصف في نفس الأمر بسلوب كثيرة فيه دفع لما يقال : تعقل السلب موقوف على ثبوت الغير فلو كان للسلب مدخل في ثبوته لدار. ووجه الدفع أن أمور كثيرة. ولا يقدح ذلك في كونه واحداً حقيقياً بحسب ذاته.
The Fifth Observatory - The Third Objective: Attribution of Multiple Effects to a Single Cause. The reality. We said: Why is it not permissible that a single essence should have a particularity with multiple things sharing in one aspect or not sharing in it, such that that particularity is not present with other things, and thus those things as a whole proceed from it, only some of them without others? And even if it is granted that there must be a particularity with each emitter specifically, that does not harm us, because the true principle is described in reality with many negations (سلوب), rather it has a will whose attachments are multiple, so it is possible that [effects] proceed from it from these aspects. His statement: (And even if) he grants (etc. is a repetition of what he mentioned in his statement: And let it not be confused with you etc. And if he said: That does not benefit you, because the true principle is described in reality with many negations, then this judgment would be a useless speech, it would be another answer. His statement: with many negations does not address the relations (إضافات); because it is possible to discuss it that the branch is the realization of the two sides, and nothing is realized with it, neither mentally nor externally. And what was said: that if His essence, exalted, is considered in a rank, then at that time neither negation nor existence is possible, and the discussion about that He, exalted, in this rank does not produce two things is pure illusion; because this consideration is a supposition of the thing without what His essence requires. And then nothing proceeds from it; due to the impossibility of its existence by this consideration. For His essence, exalted, necessarily entails in reality negations such that His existence and determination are not additional to Him, and that He is not a substance nor an accident, even if the judgment of its necessity depends on intellection. So considering it stripped of them is an impossible supposition entailing the impossible, which is the impossibility of the production of any effect from it. So reflect, for it is among what is hidden from peoples. The indications and it is refuted: that if this judgment is carried on what is understood from the expressions used for it, then there is no dispute about its closeness to clarity, because if one considers the absolute unity in which there is no multiplicity in it or with it in any way, even by multiplicity of receptacles, then the production of multiplicity is inconceivable. But this would be a useless judgment with no benefit at all; because the one in this sense is not true of any thing, neither externally nor mentally, except by way of supposition. Rather, people's dispute is frequent concerning the true one, who is God exalted, as He is in reality in His states after descending [to the level of discussion], and conceding that He is necessary by essence and that He has no existing attributes: is it permissible that a multiplicity should proceed from Him or not? So we say: Yes, why not, seeing that He has an absolute existence additional to His essence according to the philosophers as well? His statement: (We said: Why is it not permissible for a single essence etc.) And even if granted, why is it not permissible that for the simple agent, with one of its effects, there is a particularity of the production of this from it, and a particularity with another, and so on. Thus all contingent things are attributed to God exalted by this way, not as the philosophers said, well-known from them, of the events of the elemental world [proceeding] to the active intellect and the attribution of some intellects and spheres to another intellect, as will be detailed later. His statement: (Because the true principle is described in reality with many negations) contains a refutation of what is said: The intellection of negation depends on the existence of the other; if negation had an input in its existence, there would be circularity. And the face of the refutation is that many things. And that does not detract from it being truly one in terms of its essence.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the claim that a single simple cause can produce multiple effects. The author begins by stating the objection: how can a single essence have a particularity with multiple things? He then gives multiple responses. First, the essence can have special relations with different things, so that from different aspects it produces different effects. Even if each effect requires a specific relation, the essence is described in reality with many negative attributes (سلوب)—such as that its existence is not additional to it, that it is not a substance or accident—and it has a will with multiple attachments, allowing production from these aspects. He then addresses the claim that considering the essence in a rank of absolute unity would prevent multiplicity, arguing that such a consideration is a mere supposition and not real; the essence necessarily entails negations in reality. He also refutes the charge of circularity: the conceptualization of a negation depends on the conceptualization of the negated thing, not on its external existence, so negation does not need a pre-existing opposite. The passage concludes that all these negations and relations do not detract from God being truly one in essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negations in God (like 'not a substance') require the real existence of substances in order to be meaningful.
Correction: The text clarifies that only the conceptualization of the negated thing is needed, not its external existence. Thus, negations are meaningful without implying circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the consideration of God in a rank of absolute unity (no negation, no relation) an invalid objection?
A) Because God cannot be considered at all.
B) Because such a consideration is a mere supposition that ignores essential aspects of His essence.
C) Because the philosophers proved that absolute unity is impossible.
D) Because God has no negations.

Key Takeaway
The objection based on absolute unity is a mere supposition; negations are real aspects of God that do not require external existence of opposites, and they do not undermine His true oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the absolute unity of God, do you ever fall into the error of thinking He is so simple that He cannot relate to your daily struggles? How does knowing that negations do not require external existence change how you approach God's involvement in your life?
?
The philosophers claimed that God, being absolutely one, cannot produce multiplicity. In your own life, do you sometimes feel that God is too distant or too 'one-dimensional' to care about the many details of your existence? How does Jurjani's concept of particularity (special relationship with each effect) challenge that feeling?
?
Negations of God (e.g., He is not a substance, not an accident) are often seen as abstract theology. But reflect: if God were a substance or accident, what would that mean for your worship? How does affirming these negations deepen your sense of His transcendence and your own dependence on Him?

Refuting the Contradiction Claim: Producing (a) and (b)
Jurjani refutes the claim that producing (a) and producing (b) from the same source is contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why producing (a) and producing (b) from a single source is not logically contradictory, and how the argument avoids circularity.

Core Idea
The contradictory of producing (a) is not producing (a), not producing (b).

The third objection claims that a single cause cannot produce both (a) and its negation, but Jurjani shows that 'non-a' here is a positive existent (b), not the absence of (a). He then addresses a subtle issue about negative attributes and circularity, demonstrating the consistency of a simple agent producing multiple effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that a single source producing both (a) and (non-a) leads to contradiction. Notice why this seems plausible.
The objection relies on the idea that the source would be both the source of (a) and not the source of (a).
2
Identify Jurjani's key distinction
Jurjani distinguishes the contradictory of an act from a different act. The contradictory of producing (a) is its absence, not the production of (b).
Producing (b) is a positive, existent act, not the negation of producing (a).
3
Follow the handling of circularity
Read the final part where Jurjani argues that negative attributes do not imply circularity. They depend on conceptualization, not external existence.
The charge of circularity is invalid because the specific negation is not the cause of the production.

Source Text

والجواب عن الثالث : لا نسلم أن صدور (۱) و صدور ( لا (ا) تناقض، فإن نقيض صدور (ا)، وهو لا صدور (ا) وأما صدور لا (ا) أعني صدور (ب)، (فلا يناقضه ) . فإن قيل : التناقض لازم ؛ لأن الجهة التي هي مصدر لـ (ا) إن كانت مصدراً لغير (ا) صدق أن هذه الجهة ليست مصدراً لـ (ا)؛ لأن الموجبة لا . قوله : ( أعني صدور (ب)) أشار إلى دفع مناقشة وهي أن صدور لا (۱) ليس إلا عدم صدور (۱) إذ لا صدور للإعدام فيكون مناقضاً لصدور (۱)، بأن صدور لا (۱) عبارة عن صدور (ب) الموصوف بأنه لا (۱) وهو موجود. قوله : ( صدق أن هذه الجهة إلخ) ليس المراد بالمصدرية هاهنا الخصوصية السابقة على وجود المعلول، كما في الاستدلال الأول، حتى يرد عليه منع صدق أن هذه الجهة ليست مصدراً د (ا) ؛ لأن المفروض صدور (ا) و (ب) من جهة واحدة في المعنى الإضافي، ولا شك أنه إذا تعدد الصادر يكون صدور أحدهما غير صدور الآخر، فيصدق أن صدور أحدهما ليس صدور الآخر؛ لأن سلب الغير عن الشيء ضروري فيصدق أن هذه الجهة مصدر لـ (ا)، لفرض صدور عنها، وأنها ليست مصدرا له لفرض صدور غير (ا) الذي هو مستلزم لسلب صدور (۱) فیلزم التناقض، بخلاف ما إذا تعددت الجهة فإنه يدفع التناقض، فمعنى قوله : لأن الموجبة المعدولة. الاتصاف بالسلوب في نفس الأمر، وهذا الاتصاف لا يتوقف على ثبوت الغير. وأما صحة العلم بالاتصاف اللازمة له فبعد تسليم اللزوم إنما يتوقف على تصور الغير المسلوب، لا على ثبوته، منشاً على أنه لو سلم ما ذكره فإنما يلزم الدور إذا جعل السلب المخصوص لصدور المسلوب بهذا السلب وإلا فيجوز أن يوجد الفاعل البسيط شيئاً لم يعرض له سلب هذا الشيء عنه، ولم يكن هذا السلب منشأ لإيجاد شيء آخر لا بد لنفيه من دليل. فلا دور اصلاً.
And the answer to the third [objection]: we do not accept that the production of (a) and the production of (non-a) are contradictory. For the contradictory of the production of (a) is the non-production of (a). As for the production of (non-a), I mean the production of (b), it does not contradict it. For if it is said: contradiction is necessary because the source that is the source for (a), if it is a source for other than (a), then it is true that this source is not a source for (a), because the affirmative [judgment] of the negated predicate ... His statement: (I mean the production of (b)) refers to the refutation of a discussion, namely that the production of (non-a) is nothing but the absence of the production of (a), since there is no production of non-existents, so it would contradict the production of (a). [But] that the production of (non-a) is an expression for the production of (b) described as being (non-a), and it is existent. His statement: (It is true that this source etc.) The intended meaning of 'source' here is not the particularity preceding the existence of the effect, as in the first inference, such that the denial that this source is a source for (a) would be objected to; because it is assumed that (a) and (b) proceed from one source in the relational sense. And there is no doubt that when the produced is multiple, the production of one is not the production of the other, so it is true that the production of one is not the production of the other; because the negation of the other from a thing is necessary. So it is true that this source is a source for (a), because of the assumption of its production from it, and that it is not a source for it because of the assumption of the production of other than (a) which entails the negation of the production of (a), so contradiction follows. Unlike when the source is multiple, for it removes the contradiction. So the meaning of his statement: 'because the affirmative of the negated predicate...' The description with negations is in reality, and this description does not depend on the existence of the other. As for the soundness of the knowledge of the necessary description, after conceding the necessity, it only depends on the conceptualization of the negated thing, not on its existence, based on: if what he mentioned is granted, then the circularity only occurs if the specific negation is made the cause for the production of the negated thing by this negation. Otherwise, it is possible that the simple agent produces something without being subject to the negation of this thing, and this negation is not the source for the creation of something else that requires proof for its negation. So there is no circularity at all.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection. The objection argued that if a single source produces both (a) and (non-a), then contradiction arises because the source would be both the source of (a) and not the source of (a). The answer clarifies that the contradictory of producing (a) is not producing (a), not producing (b). Producing (b) is a positive act, not the negation of producing (a). The author then addresses an objection that the same source being a source for both implies a contradiction in the judgment; the commentary explains that this contradiction would only occur if the source is considered in exactly the same respect. Since production has multiple aspects, no contradiction. Finally, the author returns to the topic of negative attributes (سلوب) and circularity: the attribution of negations to God is real, but it depends only on conceptualization, not on external existence, so the charge of circularity is invalid.

Correction: Jurjani shows that the contradictory of producing (a) is not producing (a), not producing (b). 'Non-a' here is a positive existent (b), so both productions are positive acts and can coexist.

Checkpoint
Why is producing (a) and producing (b) not a contradiction according to the answer?
A) Because (a) and (b) are the same thing.
B) Because the contradictory of producing (a) is not producing (a), not producing (b).
C) Because (b) is a non-existent.
D) Because the source cannot produce both.

Key Takeaway
Producing (a) and producing (b) are both positive acts; only the absence of producing (a) contradicts its production, not the production of a different thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face circumstances that seem contradictory—like a good God allowing suffering—do you assume contradiction before understanding? How does this lesson's method of distinguishing 'not producing a' from 'producing non-a' help you avoid false contradictions in your own understanding of God's actions? Commit to pausing before accusing God of inconsistency.
?
The objector thought that one source cannot produce two different things without contradiction. Consider a time when you felt that God gave you one thing but withheld another, and you questioned His consistency. How does knowing that 'producing a' and 'producing b' are both positive acts from the same Source reshape your expectations of His provision? Will you consciously reinterpret your circumstances as complementary rather than contradictory?
?
This lesson teaches that negations attributed to God do not require the existence of the negated thing. Is there an area where you have assumed God's 'not doing' something implies a lack or deficiency? Can you find peace in trusting that His non-action is not a failure but a deliberate choice? What will you do today to surrender that assumption?
