The True Simple Has No Multiplicity
Defines the true simple and refutes the Ash'ari view of multiple real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the true simple (al-basīṭ al-ḥaqīqī) cannot be both agent and recipient from one respect, and how this refutes the Ash‘arī position that Allah has real attributes superadded to His essence.

Core Idea
The true simple has no multiplicity—not even from different respects.

This lesson establishes a key axiom in Islamic metaphysics: the true simple (like Allah) is absolutely one. The philosophers argue that such a being cannot be both the source and the recipient of an effect from the same perspective. This directly challenges the Ash‘arī claim that Allah has multiple real attributes that both proceed from Him and subsist in Him. Let us examine the precise argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of the true simple and the refutation of the Ash‘arīs.
Pay attention to the phrase 'from one respect' – it is crucial.
2
Understand the key qualification
Focus on the phrase 'from one respect'. Why is it necessary? The text implies that from two respects or for two different things, being both agent and recipient is possible.
3
Identify the Ash‘arī position
What do the Ash‘arīs hold about God's attributes? They say He has real attributes superadded to His essence. How does this conflict with the definition of the true simple?
The attributes both proceed from Him and subsist in Him, making Him both agent and recipient from the same respect.
4
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Some think that absolute simplicity denies all attributes entirely. How does the text address this? It specifies that the denial is from one respect, allowing attributes from different aspects.
The misconception is that simplicity means no attributes at all, but the text clarifies it is about being both agent and recipient from one respect.
5
Summarize the argument
In your own words, explain why the true simple cannot be both agent and recipient from one respect, and why this refutes the Ash‘arī view.
You may check the commentary for additional clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : البسيط الحقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً
عن ( قال الحكماء : البسيط) الحقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً، كالواجب تعالى، (لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً ) أي لا يكون مصدراً لأثر، وقابلاً له من جهة واحدة، خلافاً للأشاعرة، حيث ذهبوا إلى أن لله تعالى صفات حقيقية زائدة على ذاته وهي صادرة عنه وقائمة
قوله : ( لا تعدد فيه أصلاً لا من حيث الذات، ولا من حيث الصفات والاعتبارات . قوله : (أي لا يكون إلخ) أي ليس المراد عدم كونه فاعلاً وقابلاً مطلقاً، كما يفيده ظاهر المتن، بل بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد من جهة واحدة وأما بالنسبة إلى شيئين، أو إلى شيء واحد من جهتين فجائز لأنه على كلا التقديرين يجوز تقدم كونه مصدراً للقبول، أو الفعل على الآخر، فلا يلزم كون البسيط الحقيقي مصدراً لأثرين بخلاف ما نحن فيه . ومن هذا ظهر أن ما قيل : إنه لو تم الدليل الأول لدل على امتناع كون الواحد قابلاً لأمر وفاعلاً لآخر بل ينفي القبولين أيضاً مع أن مذهبهم بخلافه وهم. قوله : (حيث ذهبوا إلخ) فإنه في مرتبة الذات ليس بشيء من الصفات والاعتبارات فالواجب تعالى في تلك المرتبة واحد حقيقي، فما قيل : إن هذا مبني على عدم اعتبار السلوب، وإلا ففيه تعدد جهات الصدور ولو بالنسبة إلى الصفات وهم . :قوله : ( وإن قيدت إحداهما بالدوام كانت كاذبة فيه منع ظاهر؛ لأن فعل الواجب المفروض سرمدي فإذا صدر عنه (ا) يجب أن يقيد بالدوام فكيف يقال : إن القضيتين المذكورتين مطلقتان . قوله : ( من جهة واحدة تصريح بما علم التزاماً؛ إذ البسيط الحقيقي لا يكون إلا ذا جهة واحدة، وتوطئة لرد جواب المصنف الذي سيذكره . قوله: (خلافاً للأشاعرة حيث ذهبوا إلخ) هذا مبني على عدم اعتبار السلوب، وإلا ففيه تعدد جهات الصدور، ولو بالنسبة إلى الصفات كما نبهت عليه فيما مضى.
The Fourth Aim: The True Simple has no multiplicity at all. From (The philosophers said: The simple) the true simple has no multiplicity at all, like the Necessary Exalted, (it is not both recipient and agent) i.e., it is not a source for an effect and a recipient of it from one respect, contrary to the Ash'aris, for they held that Allah has real attributes superadded to His Essence, proceeding from Him and subsisting [in Him]. His words: (has no multiplicity at all) neither in terms of essence, nor in terms of attributes and considerations. His words: (i.e., it is not etc.) means that the intention is not absolute denial of its being agent and recipient, as the apparent meaning of the text gives, but rather in relation to one thing from one respect. As for two things, or one thing from two respects, it is permissible, because in both cases it is permissible for its being a source for reception or action to be prior to the other, so it does not necessitate the true simple being a source for two effects, contrary to our case. From this it appears that what was said – that if the first proof were valid it would prove the impossibility of one thing being recipient of one thing and agent for another, and even negates both receptions, while their school is otherwise – is a delusion. His words: (for they held etc.) He says that at the level of essence, it is not any of the attributes and considerations, so the Necessary Exalted at that level is truly one. So what was said – that this is based on not considering negations, otherwise there would be multiplicity of aspects of emanation even with respect to attributes – is a delusion. His words: (And if one of them is quantified with perpetuity it would be false) – in it there is an obvious denial, because the act of the supposed necessary is everlasting; so when (a) issues from it, it must be quantified with perpetuity; so how can it be said that the two mentioned propositions are absolute? His words: (from one respect) is a specification of what is known implicitly, since the true simple is only with one respect, and a preface for refuting the answer of the author which he will mention. His words: (contrary to the Ash'aris where they held etc.) This is based on not considering negations; otherwise there is multiplicity of aspects of emanation even with respect to attributes, as I warned earlier.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth aim: the true simple (al-basīṭ al-ḥaqīqī) such as the Necessary Existent has no multiplicity at all. It cannot be both an agent (fā‘il) and a recipient (qābil) of an effect from one respect. This is opposed to the Ash‘ari position that Allah has real attributes (ṣifāt ḥaqīqiyya) superadded to His essence, which both proceed from Him and inhere in Him. The commentator then explains the glosses: the first clarifies that the denial of multiplicity is from every aspect, not just essence. The second explains that the statement 'it is not both agent and recipient' is not an absolute denial but relative to one thing from one respect. From two respects or for two things, it is permissible. The third gloss rejects the claim that the argument would negate even two receptions. The fourth gloss explains that the phrase 'contrary to the Ash'aris' is based on ignoring God’s negative attributes (salūb); otherwise there would be multiple aspects of emanation even for attributes. The commentator also mentions a gloss about perpetual quantification, stating that the act of the Necessary is eternal, so if (a) issues from Him, it must be perpetual, contradicting the claim that the two propositions are absolute. Finally, he notes that the specification 'from one respect' is a necessary condition for the true simplicity.

Key Terms
بسيط حقيقي
قابل
فاعل
صفات حقيقية
سلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the denial of multiplicity means God cannot have any attributes at all.
Correction: The passage specifies that the denial is from one respect (جهة واحدة). Attributes can be considered from different aspects without affecting simplicity. The true simple cannot be both agent and recipient of the same effect from the same perspective, but it can be agent from one perspective and recipient from another, or agent and recipient for different things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Ash'ari view of real attributes problematic?
A) Because it implies multiplicity in the divine essence.
B) Because attributes are not real.
C) Because the Necessary cannot be an agent.
D) Because the Ash'aris deny negative attributes.

Key Takeaway
The true simple (like Allah) is absolutely one and cannot be both the source and recipient of the same effect from the same respect, which refutes the Ash'ari position of multiple real attributes superadded to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes like power or knowledge, do you ever imagine them as separate 'parts' added to His essence? How does the truth that Allah is a simple being with no multiplicity challenge that mental picture, and how can you train your heart to worship Him as absolutely one?
?
Have you ever prayed to Allah asking for something while also feeling that He is the source of that thing and the one who receives your request—but from the same respect? How does understanding the distinction between agent and recipient from one respect vs. two respects change the way you approach Him in du'a?
?
What assumptions about God's nature do you hold that might subtly imply multiplicity or composition, even if you would never say it out loud? Will you commit to examining your own understanding of tawhid and actively replace any divided view of Allah with the pure simplicity taught in this lesson?

The Truly Simple: Agent and Recipient
Author states the problem and gives an initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological response to the objection that a simple being cannot be both agent and recipient without implying multiplicity.

Core Idea
Reception and action are mental considerations, not external existents.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection: if a truly simple being were both agent and recipient, wouldn't that produce two effects from one source? The response is subtle—it redefines what we mean by 'reception' and 'action'. These are not external things adding multiplicity; they are ways we understand the same simple reality. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note that the passage is composed of two parts: a statement of the problem and an initial response.
The phrasing is dense—take your time to parse it.
2
Identify the problem
The problem states: If the truly simple were both recipient (قابل) and agent (فاعل), it would be the source for two effects (أثران). This contradicts the simplicity of the One.
Notice the phrase 'فقد صدر عن الواحد الحقيقي أثران' — two effects from the one.
3
Understand the response
The response says: Reception and action (in the sense of effecting) are not among external existents (موجودات خارجية). They are mental concepts, not real, added attributes. Thus, no multiplicity arises.
The key is that 'أثر' here means an external effect, not a mental consideration.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this response preserves divine simplicity without denying God's attributes. The attributes are not extra 'things' but ways of understanding the same simple essence.
This anticipates the doctrine that divine attributes are not additional to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الرابع : البسيط الحقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً. به ( وإلا ) وإن لم يكن كذلك، بل كان قابلاً وفاعلاً ، فهو مصدر للقبول والفعل معاً، فقد صدر عن الواحد الحقيقي أثران، وقد تبين لك بطلانه . قلنا : ( وقد عرفت ) أن القبول والفعل بمعنى التأثير ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية.
The fifth observatory - fourth objective: The truly simple has no multiplicity essentially. By it (or else) if it is not like that, but rather was recipient and agent, then it is a source for reception and action together, so two effects have proceeded from the truly one, and its falsity has become clear to you. We said: And you have known that reception and action in the sense of effect are not among external existents.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the issue and gives an initial response. The author states the problem: if the simple were both recipient (قابل) and agent (فاعل), it would be the source for two effects, implying multiplicity. But he responds by asserting that the concepts of reception and action (in the sense of effect) are not extra-mental realities (موجودات خارجية); they are mental considerations. Thus no real multiplicity arises from the simple being described as both.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God is both agent and recipient, He must have two real aspects or parts.
Correction: Reception and action are not external, real attributes; they are mental concepts applied to the one simple essence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The truly simple can be described as both agent and recipient without implying multiplicity because these descriptions are mental, not external realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How can realizing that attributes are not external existents help you take responsibility for your actions and draw closer to the One who has no multiplicity?
?
If Allah's oneness is so pure that even agency and receptivity are not real distinctions, what does that demand of your heart when you call upon Him as 'the Hearing, the Seeing'? Will you let that truth transform the sincerity of your dua tonight?

The Incompatibility of Necessity and Possibility in One Being
Author presents an argument and answers an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relation of agent to effect is necessary and the relation of recipient to received is possible, and how the author defends their incompatibility against an objection.

Core Idea
Necessity and possibility cannot coexist in the same relation.

This lesson continues the discussion on divine attributes by examining the nature of relations. The author presents a key argument that the agent's relation to its effect is necessary, while the recipient's relation to what it receives is possible. These two relations are fundamentally different and cannot be united in a single simple being. An objector challenges this, and the author provides a decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: argument, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author claims that the relation of agent to effect is by necessity, while the relation of recipient to received is by possibility. Understand these two types of relations.
Contrast necessity (وجوب) and possibility (إمكان).
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that when both agent and recipient are taken with all necessary conditions, both necessitate their effects, so there is no difference.
This challenges the author's distinction.
4
Analyze the answer
The author responds that the agent alone can in some cases necessitate its effect without any external condition, but the recipient always requires an agent. Thus, they are fundamentally different.
Key point: agency can be independent, receptivity never is.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
If the same entity were both agent and recipient from the same aspect, it would be both necessary and possible, which is contradictory.
This supports the reasoning that a simple being cannot have both relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً (جوابه)، مع ( وأيضاً : فنسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان )، فلا يجتمعان. واعترض على هذا بأن القابل إذا أخذ وحده لم يجب معه وجود المقبول، وكما أن الفاعل وحده لا يجب معه وجود المفعول، وإذا أخذا مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود المقبول والمفعول وجب وجودهما معهما، فلا فرق إذا بينهما في الوجوب والإمكان. وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده قد يكون في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجباً لمفعوله، ولا يتصور ذلك في القابل؛ إذ لا بد من الفاعل فالفعل وحده موجب في الجملة والقبول وحده ليس بموجب أصلاً، فلو اجتمعا في شيء واحد من جهة واحدة لزم إمكان الوجوب، وامتناعه من تلك الجهة.
Also (his answer), and also: the relation of the agent to the effect is by necessity, and the relation of the recipient to the received is by possibility, so they do not combine. And an objection was raised against this: that the recipient if taken alone does not necessitate the existence of the received, just as the agent alone does not necessitate the effect, and if they are taken with all that the existence of the received and the effect depend on, then their existence is necessary with them, so there is no difference between them in necessity and possibility. And it was answered: that the agent alone may in some cases be independent necessitating its effect, and that is not conceivable in the recipient; for there must be the agent, so the act alone is necessitating in general, but the reception alone is not necessitating at all, so if they combine in one thing from one aspect, it would necessitate the possibility of necessity and its impossibility from that aspect.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument and a full objection-answer exchange. The author argues that the relation of the agent to its effect is necessary (الوجوب), while the relation of the recipient to what it receives is possible (الإمكان). These two relations are incompatible in one simple being. An objector claims that if both agent and recipient are taken with all their conditions, they both necessitate their effects, so there is no real difference. The author answers by highlighting that the agent can in some cases be independent and necessitating without any external condition, whereas the recipient always requires another agent. Hence, if the same entity were both agent and recipient from one aspect, it would be simultaneously necessary and possible with respect to the same effect—a contradiction.

Key Terms
نسبة
وجوب
إمكان
فاعل
قابل
موجب
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessity and possibility are only relative to conditions and thus can be reconciled in the same relation.
Correction: The author shows that agency can be intrinsically necessitating, while receptivity is never intrinsically necessitating, so they are fundamentally different types of relation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim the agent's relation differs from the recipient's?
A) Because the agent is always necessary.
B) Because the agent alone can in some cases necessitate its effect without external conditions.
C) Because the recipient always necessitates its effect.
D) Because both relations are equally necessary.

Key Takeaway
The agent's relation to its effect is necessary by itself, while the recipient's relation is possible; they cannot inhere in one simple being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or make duʿā’, do you ever catch yourself thinking that Allah 'needs' your worship or your requests? How does this lesson's distinction between the self-sufficient Agent and the dependent recipient change the way you approach Him? What concrete shift in your prayer attitude will you make starting today?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of your religious deeds. Did you subconsciously see yourself as both the agent (earning reward) and the recipient (of Allah's acceptance)? How does the impossibility of being both from the same aspect expose that pride? Write down one way you will remind yourself daily that you are purely the recipient and Allah alone is the Agent.

Refutation of the Two-Aspect Response
Author presents a response and its refutation regarding the simple's aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the simple cannot have two different relations from different aspects when discussing agency and receptivity.

Core Idea
A simple thing cannot have multiple aspects without losing its simplicity.

In this passage, the author presents a proposed answer to a problem about the simple (basiṭ) and then refutes it. The answer tries to separate the necessary relation from the possible relation by appealing to two different aspects. The refutation aims to show that this appeal undermines the very simplicity that was assumed. Let's examine this exchange closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation without analyzing too deeply. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage contains two main parts: a response and its refutation.
2
Identify the response and its refutation
The response claims that the simple can have two different relations (necessary and possible) from two different aspects. The refutation counters that this would require multiple aspects, contradicting the premise of simplicity.
The refutation is introduced by 'and this answer was refuted'.
3
Understand why the refutation works
The refutation points out that the discussion assumes the simple has only one aspect. If you introduce two aspects, you are essentially negating its simplicity. This is a logical critique.
The final phrase hints at another possible answer but is not elaborated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والجواب : أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون للشيء البسيط إلى شيء آخر نسبتان مختلفتان بالوجوب والإمكان من جهتين مختلفتين، فتجب النسبة الناشئة من جهة، ولا تجب النسبة الناشئة من جهة أخرى، ورد هذا الجواب : بأن كلامنا في أن البسيط لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً من جهة واحدة، وعلى ما ذكرتم تكون تلك الجهة متعددة ) ومنهم من أجاب : عن الوجه
(And the answer: that it is not impossible for the simple thing to have, with respect to another thing, two different relations by necessity and possibility from two different aspects, so the relation arising from one aspect is necessary, and the relation arising from another aspect is not necessary. And this answer was refuted: because our discussion is that the simple is not recipient and agent from one aspect, and according to what you mention, that aspect would be multiple. And from them is one who answered: concerning the face...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a third round in the debate. The advocate responds by saying that the simple can have two different relations (one necessary, one possible) from two different aspects—one as agent, one as recipient. The refuter points out that this would mean the simple has multiple aspects, contradicting its absolute simplicity. The phrase 'and from them is one who answered concerning the face' hints at another possible answer, but it is not elaborated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The simple can have multiple real aspects without compromising its simplicity.
Correction: The refutation shows that positing multiple aspects in the simple undermines its very definition as simple.

Checkpoint

A) The simple cannot have two different relations.
B) The simple has no multiple aspects.
C) The two aspects are contradictory.
D) The response is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The argument from two different aspects fails because it posits multiple aspects in the simple, contradicting its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often present different faces of yourself to different people, justifying it as 'different aspects of your personality.' But this lesson teaches that a simple being cannot have multiple aspects without contradiction. Are you willing to be one person, fully consistent in your character before God and others, even if it means being vulnerable and losing the safety of multiple roles?
?
When you fail to live up to your ideals, do you excuse yourself by thinking you are simply 'acting from a different aspect' of your nature? The refutation of the two-aspect response calls you to account: you are a single being. How will you take responsibility for the unity of your actions before the One who sees you as you truly are?
